The reason for istihsan is that it entails hardship; for Muslims need to teach and learn the Quran, and they often need repetition, so requiring repetition leads to hardship, which is repelled. It is supported by what is related that Jibril used to recite to the Prophet and his Companions, and they would not prostrate except once, and similarly from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami, teacher of al-Hasan and al-Husayn, as in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. This overlapping is overlapping in the cause: by making all as one recitation with the condition of unity of sitting and verse, and therefore one suffices for what was before and what came after. If it were overlapping in the ruling only, it would not suffice except for what was before.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on sujud al-tilawah (prostration of Quranic recitation) in the Hanafi school, explained by the commentator. The ruling states that if a person recites a sajdah verse and then repeats it in the same sitting (majlis), only one prostration is required, whether the repetition occurs before or after the initial prostration. The commentator clarifies that the repetition is not necessarily within prayer; the first recitation may be outside prayer and the second inside. He criticizes al-Birjandi's interpretation that the repetition itself occurs in prayer. The key basis for this ruling is istihsan (juristic preference) which alleviates the hardship that would arise from requiring multiple prostrations for repeated recitations, as Muslims commonly repeat verses for teaching and learning. This istihsan is supported by the practice of Jibril reciting to the Prophet and the Companions prostrating only once. The principle of tadakhul (overlapping) is applied here: the multiple recitations are treated as one due to the unity of the sitting and the verse. This is tadakhul in the cause (sabab), meaning that one prostration serves for all recitations in that sitting. If it were tadakhul in the ruling alone, the later prostration would not cover earlier recitations.

Key Terms
التداخل (al-tadakhul): The principle that multiple causes of an act may be satisfied by a single performance of that act.
المجلس (al-majlis): The sitting or place where one is engaged in recitation; unity of place and time is required for tadakhul.
سجدة التلاوة (sajdat al-tilawah): The prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of prostration in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone recites a sajdah verse twice in the same sitting, how many prostrations are required?
A) Two prostrations, one for each recitation.
B) One prostration suffices due to tadakhul.
C) It depends on whether the recitations were inside or outside prayer.
D) No prostration is required if the recitations are in the same sitting.

Key Takeaway
One prostration suffices for multiple recitations of the same sajdah verse in one sitting, based on the Hanafi principles of tadakhul and istihsan.

Change of Verse or Sitting: When Separate Prostrations Become Required
Ruling with proof for sujud al-tilawah upon change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a change of sajdah verse or sitting requires a separate prostration, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding repetition in different rak'ahs.

Core Idea
Change of verse or sitting breaks unity; each requires its own wajib prostration.

Sujud al-tilawah is generally required upon reciting or hearing a sajdah verse. But what if you recite two different verses, or the same verse in a different sitting? This passage clarifies that a single prostration cannot cover multiple distinct causes. We will explore the rule, examples like the weaver, and an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on repeating the same verse in different rak'ahs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
Read the opening: 'If he changes it (the verse) or the sitting, no [one prostration does not suffice].' State the rule: changing the verse (different sajdah verse) or the sitting (different location/session) invalidates tadakhul (merging), requiring a separate prostration for each.
Note: 'no' means one prostration is not enough.
2
Recognize examples of changing sitting
The text gives examples: a weaver moving back and forth (isda' al-thawb) and moving from branch to branch. Understand that these changes of physical place break the unity of the majlis (sitting), so each occurrence demands its own prostration.
The commentary also mentions threshing, going around a mill, swimming – all involve moving place.
3
Apply the rule to repetition in one rak'ah
If the same verse is repeated within the same rak'ah, there is consensus that one prostration suffices, whether you prostrate first and then repeat, or repeat and then prostrate. The sitting is considered one.
No ikhtilaf here.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf for different rak'ahs
Now consider repeating the same verse in two different rak'ahs. Abu Hanifa and the later view of Abu Yusuf hold, based on qiyas, that no additional prostration is needed because the prayer's continuity makes it one sitting. Muhammad al-Shaybani, based on istihsan, says another prostration is required because each rak'ah is a separate sitting. The commentaries note that the view of Abu Hanifa is considered more correct.
This is a practical difference: following Abu Hanifa, you don't repeat; following Muhammad, you do.
5
Extend the rule to the listener
The listener's obligation is tied to his own sitting. If the listener changes place, his sitting changes, so he must prostrate again. Example: a rider praying and a walking servant – the servant's obligation repeats, the rider's does not.
This shows the rule applies to both reciter and listener, but based on their own sitting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he changes it or the sitting, no [one prostration does not suffice]. And weaving of cloth, and moving from one branch to another branch: a change, and another becomes obligatory. So on this basis, if he repeats it in one rak'ah, one prostration suffices, whether he prostrated then repeated, or repeated then prostrated. And if he repeats in another rak'ah, one prostration suffices — this according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. (And if he changes it): i.e., the sajdah verse, or the sitting, so: i.e., if he recites two verses in one sitting, or one verse in two sittings, one prostration does not suffice. (And weaving of cloth, and moving from one branch to another branch is a change). Weaving of cloth is that the weaver fixes sticks in the ground on which he arranges the warp of the cloth as he goes back and forth, so his sitting changes by moving from place to place. (And another becomes obligatory): i.e., upon the listener. [1] His statement: 'so on this basis...'; al-Birjandi said: If he repeats it in one rak'ah, one prostration suffices by agreement. But if he recites it in one rak'ah and prostrates, then repeats it in another, in qiyas another is not necessary, and that is the view of Abu Hanifa and the other view of Abu Yusuf, which is the most correct, while in istihsan another is necessary. As in al-Khulasah. (2) His statement: 'in a rak'ah'; if he repeats in the second pair after reciting in the first pair and prostrating, another becomes obligatory by agreement, as in al-Qunyah. [3] His statement: 'and weaving of cloth'; al-Tamurtashi said: There is disagreement concerning the weaving of cloth, threshing, the one going around the mill, the one swimming in water, and the one moving from branch to branch. The most correct is that obligation is repeated due to change of sitting, as in Fath al-Qadir. [4] His statement: 'i.e., upon the listener'; as for the second [reciter], one suffices due to unity of his sitting. So if he repeats it while riding and praying, and his servant is walking, the obligation is repeated for the servant but not for the rider. If the person praying on an animal in one place repeats it many times, obligation is one in his case, but multiple for his companion due to difference of place for the listener.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage continues the rulings on sujud al-tilawah, now discussing cases where the unity of verse or sitting is broken. The rule is: if the verse changes (different sajdah verse) or the sitting changes (different session), a single prostration does not suffice; each requires its own prostration. The commentator illustrates change with examples like a weaver moving back and forth (changing sitting) or a person moving from one branch to another (changing location). The ruling also covers the listener: if the listener hears a repetition in a different place, he must prostrate again. The commentary then addresses a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if a person recites in one rak'ah and prostrates, then recites the same verse in another rak'ah, what is the ruling? According to qiyas (and the view of Abu Hanifa and the later view of Abu Yusuf), no additional prostration is required because it is still the same verse and the same prayer. But according to istihsan (and the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani), another prostration is required because the rak'ahs constitute different sittings. The commentator notes that al-Birjandi reports agreement in the case of one rak'ah. However, if the repetition is in the second pair (of a four-rak'ah prayer) after the first pair, then another prostration is required by agreement. The commentary on the listener distinguishes between the reciter (on a moving animal) whose sitting is unified, and the listener (walking) whose sitting changes, so the obligation repeats for the listener.

Key Terms
البدل (al-badal): Change, either of the verse (reciting a different sajdah verse) or of the sitting (changing location).
إسداء الثوب (isda' al-thawb): Weaving cloth: the weaver moves back and forth, thus changing his sitting place.
السامع (al-sami'): The listener who hears a sajdah verse; he must prostrate if he hears it, and his obligation is tied to his own sitting.
التداخل (al-tadakhul): Merging of multiple causes into a single act; when causes are unified, one prostration covers all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (al-asahh) in the Hanafi school, if a person recites a sajdah verse in the first rak'ah, prostrates, then recites the same verse in the second rak'ah, what is the ruling?
A) He must prostrate again because it is a new rak'ah.
B) No additional prostration is required based on qiyas.
C) He must prostrate twice, once for each recitation.
D) He must repeat the prayer.

Key Takeaway
A change in either the sajdah verse or the physical sitting breaks unity, requiring a separate prostration; within the same prayer, the mufta bihi view does not require an additional prostration for repeating the same verse in a different rak'ah.

Repetition of Sujud al-Tilawah in a New Rak'ah: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repetition in another rak'ah involving qiyas and istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding repetition of the same ayah al-sajdah in a different rak'ah, and evaluate why istihsan is preferred as the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Repeating the same sajdah verse in a new rak'ah: qiyas says no new sujud, but istihsan requires one because each rak'ah is a new session.

The Hanafi school has an internal dispute about whether reciting the same verse of prostration in two different rak'ahs requires two separate sujud. This lesson unpacks the three imams' views, the role of qiyas vs istihsan, and the final fatwa. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists prioritize equity over strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
State the problem
The question: if a person recites the same ayah al-sajdah in the first rak'ah and then again in the second rak'ah of a prayer, does a single sujud al-tilawah suffice, or must he perform another?
2
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa (and one view of Abu Yusuf) holds by qiyas that no new sujud is required. Muhammad holds that a new sujud is required. Another view of Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad by istihsan.
The passage attributes the qiyas view to Abu Hanifa and the istihsan view to Muhammad, with Abu Yusuf having both a qiyas and an istihsan view.
3
Understand the qiyas argument
By qiyas, the obligation of sujud al-tilawah is tied to the act of recitation. Repeating the same verse is a repetition of the same cause, so one sujud covers it, just as repeating it in one rak'ah only requires one sujud.
The qiyas basis is repetition in one rak'ah, which is agreed to require only one sujud.
4
Understand the istihsan argument
By istihsan, the second rak'ah constitutes a new session (majlis). Since sujud al-tilawah is wajib per session, the change of rak'ah triggers a new obligation. This is preferred because it aligns with the principle that each session is independent.
The passage states that the second rak'ah is a new session, so the obligation repeats.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view (fatwa) is that a new sujud is required. This is the istihsan view, adopted by Muhammad and the other view of Abu Yusuf, and supported by later authorities like al-Birjandi and al-Khulasa.
The tarjih is based on the principle that the ruling follows the session.

Source Text

And it was declared sound in al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyyah that there is no repetition upon the listener because the cause with respect to him is hearing. He said in al-Yanabigh: upon it is the fatwa. [3] His statement: 'know... etc.'; he said in Halbat al-Mujalli: 'The principle is that the obligation does not repeat except by one of three matters: difference of recitation, or hearing, or session. As for the first two: what is meant by them is difference of what is recited and what is heard, such that if he recited all the prostrations of the Quran or heard them in one session or in sessions, all are obligatory. As for the last one, it is of two types: Real (change): by moving from it to another by more than two steps, as in many books, or by more than three, as in al-Muhit, as long as the two places do not have the ruling of one, like the mosque, the house, the ship even if moving, and the desert with respect to the reciter in prayer while riding. And virtual (change): that is by undertaking an action that is considered in custom as a cutting off of what preceded; such as if he recited then ate a lot, or slept lying down, or breastfed her child, or engaged in selling or buying or marriage, contrary to if his sitting or recitation was long, or he glorified or said tahlil, or ate a morsel, or drank a sip, or slept
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings regarding repetition of sujud al-tilawah (prostration of recitation) and the effect of changing the majlis (session/place). The author begins by stating that if one changes the verse of prostration or the session, a single prostration does not suffice; rather, another becomes obligatory. Examples include a weaver whose position changes as he moves, or someone moving from branch to branch. The principle is that each distinct session requires a separate prostration.

Imam Abu Yusuf holds that repeating the same verse in a different rak'ah still requires only one prostration, while Imam Muhammad holds that each new rak'ah necessitates a new prostration. According to al-Birjandi, there is consensus that repeating in the same rak'ah only requires one prostration. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in one view) adhere to qiyas (analogy) that no additional prostration is required, but the relied-upon view (istihsan) is that another prostration is obligatory.

The discussion then turns to the listener: if the listener changes his session (e.g., walks to another place), the obligation of prostration repeats for him. However, if only the reciter changes his session while the listener remains, the obligation does not repeat for the listener according to the fatwa (as in al-Yanabigh), because the cause for the listener is hearing, not recitation.

The passage concludes with a detailed principle: the obligation only repeats due to one of three factors: difference in the recited verse, difference in what is heard, or change of session. Change of session can be real (physical movement beyond two or three steps) or virtual (engaging in an act that customarily breaks continuity, like eating a large amount or starting a new transaction). Minor acts like eating a morsel or saying tahlil do not constitute a change of session.

Key Terms
سجود التلاوة (sujud al-tilawah): The prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a specific verse of prostration in the Quran. It is wajib according to the Hanafi school.
المجلس (al-majlis): The place or session in which one recites or hears a verse of prostration. Changes of majlis affect whether a new sujud is required.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference; a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling. In this passage, istihsan requires a new sujud when repeating in a different rak'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a person recites the same ayah al-sajdah in two different rak'ahs of the same prayer?
A) One sujud suffices according to all Hanafi scholars.
B) Abu Yusuf says one sujud suffices; Muhammad says a second sujud is required; the fatwa is that a second sujud is required.
C) Abu Hanifa says two sujud are required; Abu Yusuf agrees.
D) No sujud is required because the recitation is in prayer.

Key Takeaway
When repeating the same sajdah verse in a different rak'ah, the mufta bihi requires a new sujud because the change of rak'ah constitutes a change of session, overriding the qiyas of one sujud.

Change of Majlis: Real vs Virtual and Effect on Listener
Definition of change of majlis (real and virtual) and its effect on sujud al-tilawah for reciter and listener.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and virtual change of majlis and apply the ruling that the obligation of sujud al-tilawah repeats upon a change of session, and identify when the listener's obligation repeats independently.

Core Idea
Sujud al-tilawah repeats only with a change of session—either real (physical movement) or virtual (an act breaking continuity).

Beyond the specific dispute of rak'ah repetition, the broader principle is that sujud al-tilawah is tied to the session (majlis). This lesson defines what constitutes a change of session: real movement, virtual acts (like eating a lot, sleeping, engaging in a new transaction), and how these affect both the reciter and the listener. Understanding the exact boundaries prevents either omission or excessive repetition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three factors causing repetition
The obligation repeats only due to: (1) change of recited verse, (2) change of heard verse (i.e., different verse), or (3) change of session. Focus on (3).
The first two are straightforward: different verse = different sujud.
2
Define real change of majlis
Real change is physical movement from one place to another by more than two steps (or three in some sources). Places with a unified ruling (mosque, house, ship, desert for a rider) do not count as separate sessions.
The exact number of steps varies by source (2 in many books, 3 in al-Muhit).
3
Define virtual change of majlis
Virtual change is not physical movement but engaging in an act that customarily cuts off continuity, such as eating a lot, sleeping lying down, breastfeeding, or starting a new trade transaction. Minor acts like sipping, eating a morsel, or saying tahlil do not change the session.
The standard is custom (urf): what people consider a break.
4
Apply to listener vs reciter
If the listener changes his session (e.g., walks away), his obligation repeats. If only the reciter changes session but the listener remains, the listener's obligation does not repeat according to the fatwa (al-Yanabigh). There was a difference: al-Kafi held it repeats, but al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyyah supported non-repetition.
The cause for the listener is hearing, not recitation; if he remains, the session is same for him.
5
Work through examples
Practice with real scenarios: reciter on a mount in one place repeats; his obligation is one. But his walking companion who hears in different places has multiple obligations. Similarly, a weaver at a loom changes his session with each step.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings regarding repetition of sujud al-tilawah (prostration of recitation) and the effect of changing the majlis (session/place). The author begins by stating that if one changes the verse of prostration or the session, a single prostration does not suffice; rather, another becomes obligatory. Examples include a weaver whose position changes as he moves, or someone moving from branch to branch. The principle is that each distinct session requires a separate prostration.

Imam Abu Yusuf holds that repeating the same verse in a different rak'ah still requires only one prostration, while Imam Muhammad holds that each new rak'ah necessitates a new prostration. According to al-Birjandi, there is consensus that repeating in the same rak'ah only requires one prostration. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in one view) adhere to qiyas (analogy) that no additional prostration is required, but the relied-upon view (istihsan) is that another prostration is obligatory.

The discussion then turns to the listener: if the listener changes his session (e.g., walks to another place), the obligation of prostration repeats for him. However, if only the reciter changes his session while the listener remains, the obligation does not repeat for the listener according to the fatwa (as in al-Yanabigh), because the cause for the listener is hearing, not recitation.

The passage concludes with a detailed principle: the obligation only repeats due to one of three factors: difference in the recited verse, difference in what is heard, or change of session. Change of session can be real (physical movement beyond two or three steps) or virtual (engaging in an act that customarily breaks continuity, like eating a large amount or starting a new transaction). Minor acts like eating a morsel or saying tahlil do not constitute a change of session.

Key Terms
سجود التلاوة (sujud al-tilawah): The prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a specific verse of prostration in the Quran. It is wajib according to the Hanafi school.
المجلس (al-majlis): The place or session in which one recites or hears a verse of prostration. Changes of majlis affect whether a new sujud is required.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference; a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling. In this passage, istihsan requires a new sujud when repeating in a different rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of sujud al-tilawah repeats only with a change of session, which can be real (physical movement beyond two steps) or virtual (substantial act breaking custom), and the listener's obligation depends on his own session, not the reciter's.

intermediate

Does Sajdat al-Tilawah Repeat When the Reciter's Session Changes?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repetition of sajdat al-tilawah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when sajdat al-tilawah repeats and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the listener's obligation when the reciter's session changes.

Core Idea
Sajdat al-tilawah repeats only when the session, recitation, or hearing changes; the listener's obligation is tied to hearing, not the reciter's session.

The obligation of sajdat al-tilawah (prostration of recitation) depends on three factors: the recitation itself, hearing the verse, and the continuity of the session (majlis). This lesson focuses on a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person hears a sajdah verse in one session, and later the same verse is recited again by the same or different reciter, does the obligation repeat if only the reciter's session changes, but the listener's session remains the same? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the relied-upon position and provides a thorough definition of what constitutes a change of session.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
First, locate the explicit ruling in the passage: if the listener's session changes, a new prostration is wajib; if only the reciter's session changes, no repetition for the listener. This is the basis of the discussion.
The phrase 'لا في عكسه' indicates no repetition in the opposite scenario.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the disagreement: some Hanafi scholars (as in al-Kafi) argued that the obligation repeats for the listener because recitation is the cause, though conditioned by hearing. Others (al-Hidayah, al-Khaniyyah) held that hearing itself is the direct cause, so no repetition. The fatwa is upon the latter.
This is a legitimate difference within the school, not between madhabs.
3
Understand the causes of repetition
From the Halbat al-Mujalli quote, learn the three causes: different recitation, different hearing, or different session. The first two refer to the content itself; the last is about the context of the gathering.
These three are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
4
Define real vs. legal change of session
Real change: physical movement beyond 2-3 steps (depending on the source). Legal change: an action that customarily breaks the session, such as eating a lot, sleeping lying down, or engaging in a sale. Minor actions do not break it.
The distinction is crucial for applying the rule correctly.
5
Apply the rule to special cases
Note that corners of a mosque are one place for following the imam; branches of a tree are considered different places in the apparent view. Standing does not change the session, unlike the case of the mukhayyarah.
These illustrate the precise boundaries of 'session change.'

وحكمي : وذلك بمباشرة عمل يعدُّ في العرف قطعاً لما قبله ؛ كما لو تلا ثم أكل كثيراً، أو نام مضطجعاً، أو أرضعت ولدها، أو أخذ في بيع أو شراء أو نكاح، بخلاف ما إذا طال جلوسه أو قراءته، أو سبح أو هلل ، أو أكل لقمة أو شرب شربة ، أو نام قاعداً، أو كان جالساً فقام أو مشى خطوتين أو ثلاثاً على الخلاف، أو كان قائماً فقعد، أو نازلاً فركب في مكانه فلا تكرر). انتهى ملخصاً.
أما زوايا البيت والمسجد ، ففي حكم مكان واحد بدلالة صحة الاقتداء ، وأغصانُ الشَّجرة الواحدة أمكنة مختلفة في ظاهرا وفي «النوادر»: مكان واحد. الرواية.
وبالقيام هاهنا لا يتبدل المجلس بخلاف المخيرة، فإن القيام ثمة دليل الإعراض يشبه الاستنكاف
If the listener's session changes but not the reciter's, not in its opposite): i.e., no other prostration is obligatory for the listener if the reciter's session changes without the listener's. And know that the session here changes by starting another matter, and by moving from one place to another that are not considered one in ruling. His statement: 'If the listener's session changes' means he heard an ayah in a session then that same ayah from that same reciter or another in another session. If he heard an ayah from a man then heard that same ayah from another, and recited that ayah while the session is one, one prostration suffices according to the most correct view due to the unity of the ayah and place, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: 'not in its opposite' there is disagreement: some say the obligation repeats for the listener also because recitation is the cause in his right but with the condition of hearing; and al-Kafi corrected it, while al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyyah corrected non-repetition for the listener because the cause in his right is hearing; al-Yanabi' said the fatwa is upon it. His statement: 'Know...' etc.; he said in Halbat al-Mujalli: 'The principle is that obligation does not repeat except by one of three matters: difference of recitation, or hearing, or session. As for the first two, they mean difference of what is recited and what is heard, such that if one recited all the prostration verses of the Quran or heard them in one session or multiple sessions, all are obligatory. As for the last, it is two types: Real: by moving from it to another by more than two steps, as in many books, or more than three, as in al-Muhit, provided the two places do not have the ruling of one like the mosque and house and ship even if moving, and the desert for the reciter in prayer while riding. And legal: by undertaking an action that is customarily considered cutting off what came before, such as if he recited then ate a lot, or slept lying down, or breastfed her child, or engaged in sale or purchase or marriage, unlike if his sitting or reciting was long, or he glorified or praised, or ate a morsel or drank a sip, or slept sitting, or was sitting then stood or walked two or three steps according to the difference, or was standing then sat, or dismounted then rode in his place then there is no repetition). End summarized. As for the corners of the house and mosque, they are in the ruling of one place due to the correctness of following (iqtida), and branches of one tree are different places in the apparent despite and in al-Nawadir: one place. The narration. And by standing here the session does not change contrary to the mukhayyarah, for standing there is evidence of turning away resembling disdain.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the repetition of sajdat al-tilawah when the session (majlis) changes for the listener but not the reciter, and vice versa. The author first explains the ruling: if the listener's session changes, another prostration is required; if only the reciter's session changes, no repetition. He then cites a disagreement: some scholars hold that the obligation repeats for the listener even when the reciter's session changes, because the cause for the listener is the recitation (subject to hearing). However, the relied-upon position (in al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyyah) is no repetition because the cause is the act of hearing itself, not the recitation. The fatwa is upon this latter view. Next, the passage defines 'change of session' (tabaddul al-majlis) through a quote from Halbat al-Mujalli, stating three causes for repetition: different recitation, different hearing, or different session. Session change is either real (physical movement beyond two/three steps) or legal (an action customarily cutting off the previous session, like eating a lot, sleeping lying down, or engaging in a sale). Minor actions like eating a morsel or walking a few steps do not change the session. The passage also addresses special cases: corners of a mosque are treated as one place (for following the imam), and branches of a tree are considered different places in the apparent view but one place in al-Nawadir. Finally, it notes that standing after sitting does not change the session, unlike the case of a woman given the choice of divorce (mukhayyarah), where standing indicates disinterest and nullifies her option.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The session or gathering in which the recitation or hearing occurs; it changes by starting another matter or by physical movement beyond a certain distance.
سجود التلاوة (sujud al-tilawah): The prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a specific verse of the Quran containing a sajdah.
المخيرة (al-mukhayyarah): A woman given the choice of divorce by her husband; her option remains valid until she shows disinterest or the session changes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person hears an ayah of sajdah in the mosque, then goes to another room and hears the same ayah again from the same reciter. How many prostrations are required?
A) One prostration, because the ayah and hearing are the same.
B) Two prostrations, because the session changed.
C) One prostration, because the reciter did not change.
D) Two prostrations, because the place changed.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of sajdat al-tilawah repeats for a change in session, recitation, or hearing; the listener's obligation is based on hearing, so only a change in his own session or hearing triggers repetition.

Ruling on Skipping the Sajdah Verse
Ruling statement on skipping the sajdah verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on skipping a sajdah verse and identify the type of dislike (karahah tahrimiyyah versus tanzihiyyah).

Core Idea
Skipping the verse of sajdah to avoid prostration is makruh tahrimi; reading only that verse is not disliked but praiseworthy.

In the recitation of the Quran, certain verses require prostration (sajdat al-tilawah). A common question arises: is it permissible to skip such a verse and continue reading? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on this issue, distinguishing between two opposite scenarios and explaining the underlying reasons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is deliberately skipping the verse of sajdah and reading the rest of the surah. The ruling is karahah (dislike). Determine whether it is prohibitive (tahrimi) or merely offensive (tanzihi).
The text explicitly says 'the apparent is that the dislike is prohibitive'.
2
Understand the reasons for dislike
The passage gives three reasons: abandoning a portion of the Quran (hajr al-Quran), fleeing from the act of prostration (firar min al-sajdah), and altering the composition of the Quranic text (taghyir ta'lif al-Quran).
These reasons are based on the character of believers and the sanctity of the Quran.
3
Contrast with the opposite scenario
The opposite scenario — reading only the verse of sajdah and not the rest — is not disliked. In fact, it is an act of obedience (mubadarah ila al-ta'ah) because it shows eagerness to prostrate.
The text says 'not its opposite'.
4
Note the special case inside prayer
If one recites only the verse of sajdah in prayer, confining to one verse is itself disliked (makruh) because it deprives of the sunnah of reciting more. This is an additional ruling specific to prayer.
The source cites al-Dhakhirah for this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره ترك سجدة، وقراءة باقى السورة، لا عكسه) : أي لا يكره قراءة آية السجدة
وكره ترك سجدة) : أي ترك آية السجدة، وقراءة باقي السورة؛ لأنه في ذلك هجر شيء من القرآن، وفراراً من السجدة، وذلك ليس من أخلاق المؤمنين، وأيضاً فيه تغيير تأليف القرآن، وقطع نظمه، والظاهر أن الكراهة تحريمية، كذا في «النهر».
قوله : عكسه؛ قالوا : ويجب أن يكره في الصلاة؛ لأن الاقتصار على آية واحدة في الصلاة مكروه، كذا في «الذخيرة».
أي لا يكره؛ أي لا تنزيهاً ولا تحريماً، فيه مبادرة إلى الطاعة.
And it is disliked to leave a prostration, and to read the rest of the surah, not its opposite): i.e., it is not disliked to read the verse of prostration. And it is disliked to leave a prostration: i.e., to leave the verse of prostration and read the rest of the surah, because in that is abandoning something of the Quran, and fleeing from the prostration, and that is not from the character of the believers, and also it changes the composition of the Quran and cuts its order, and the apparent is that the dislike is prohibitive, thus in al-Nahr. His statement: its opposite; they said: and it must be disliked in prayer, because restricting to one verse in prayer is disliked, thus in al-Dhakhirah. i.e., it is not disliked; neither tanzihan nor tahriman, rather it is initiating obedience.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage states a ruling of dislike (karahah) regarding sajdat al-tilawah. It is disliked (makruh) to deliberately skip the ayah of sajdah and read the rest of the surah, because this involves abandoning a portion of the Quran, fleeing from the act of prostration (which is a virtuous act), and altering the order of the Quranic text. The commentator notes that this dislike is of the prohibitive kind (karahah tahrimiyyah) according to al-Nahr. Conversely, it is not disliked to read only the verse of prostration and stop, without reading the rest of the surah; in fact, this is an act of obedience (mubadarah ila al-ta'ah) because it shows eagerness to perform the prostration. The passage also addresses the opposite scenario: if one recites the verse of prostration in prayer, it is disliked to confine oneself to just that one verse (without continuing the surah), because doing so in prayer is disliked. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between skipping the prostration verse (disliked) and reading only the prostration verse (not disliked).

Key Terms
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrimiyyah): A disliked act that is close to haram, based on a zanni evidence; omitting it is obligatory.
هجر القرآن (hajr al-Quran): Abandoning or neglecting a portion of the Quran, which is blameworthy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on reciting only the verse of sajdah without reading the rest of the surah?
A) It is makruh tahrimi.
B) It is not disliked; it is an act of obedience.
C) It is haram.
D) It is mustahab but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
Skipping the verse of sajdah and reading the rest of the surah is makruh tahrimi, while reading only the sajdah verse is commendable.

Skipping a Prostration Verse: Ruling and Etiquette
Explanation of ruling on skipping prostration verse and related etiquette

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on skipping a prostration verse while reciting the rest of the surah, explain the rationale (talil) behind it, and apply the recommended etiquette of recitation.

Core Idea
Skipping a prostration verse and reciting the rest of the surah is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) because it abandons part of the Qur'an and breaks its order.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the legal ruling on intentionally omitting a prostration verse (ayat al-sajdah) during recitation. While the prostration itself is obligatory (wajib) upon reciting or hearing such a verse, deliberately skipping it and continuing with the surah is considered makruh tahrimi. The text also provides recommended practices to avoid suspicion of preferring some verses over others and to protect listeners who may not be in a state of ritual purity (wudu).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intentionally leaving out a prostration verse and reciting the rest of the surah without prostrating. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The opposite—reciting only the prostration verse without the rest—is not disliked.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The dislike is because it involves abandoning some part of the Qur'an, fleeing from the obligatory prostration, and altering the composition and order of the Qur'anic text.
The text states: 'فيه هجر شيء من القرآن، وفراراً من السجدة' and 'تغيير تأليف القرآن، وقطع نظمه.'
3
Distinguish the opposite case
Reciting only the prostration verse (without the rest) is not disliked; it is a hastening to obedience (مبادرة إلى الطاعة).
The phrase 'لا عكسه' clarifies this.
4
Note the recommended etiquette
When reciting a prostration verse, it is recommended (mandub) to join one or two verses before it to avoid suspicion of preference. It is also preferred (mustahsan) to conceal the recitation from a listener who may not have wudu, lest the prostration become obligatory upon him and he fail to perform it.
The concealment is to prevent the listener from incurring an obligation they might neglect.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
When you recite a surah containing a prostration verse, you should include the verse and perform the prostration (or intend it later if unable). Do not intentionally skip it, as that is makruh tahrimi. If reciting aloud, consider hiding the prostration verse from an audience that may not be in wudu.
Modern application: in a Quran study circle, the reciter should ensure the prostration verse is recited and acted upon.

Source Text

Book of Prayer / Prostration of Recitation. It is disliked to leave a prostration [verse], and to recite the rest of the surah, not the opposite. As for the corners of the house and the mosque, they are in the ruling of one place on the evidence of the validity of following [the imam], and the branches of one tree are different places in the apparent [narration], and in al-Nawadir: one place. Al-Riwayah. And by standing here the session does not change contrary to the mukhayyarah, for standing there is evidence of aversion resembling disdain. (Not the opposite): i.e., it is not disliked to recite the verse of prostration. And dislike of leaving a prostration: i.e., leaving the verse of prostration, and reciting the rest of the surah; because... sitting, or if he was sitting and stood or walked two or three steps on the disagreement, or if he was standing and sat, or descending and rode in his place, it is not repeated). Ended summarized. [1] His saying: so in the ruling of one place; i.e., even though they are multiple in sense and reality, they are united in ruling, as evidenced by the validity of following from one corner to an imam in another corner, and if they were not united in ruling it would not be permissible. [2] His saying: by standing... etc.; meaning if he was sitting and recited the verse and prostrated, then stood in that same place and did not move from it, then recited it standing, only one prostration is obligatory because the session has not changed. [3] His saying: contrary to the mukhayyarah; it is a noun of object from takhyir; she is the woman whose husband gave her the choice to divorce herself and said to her: choose yourself or the like, and its ruling is that she is upon her choice as long as the session has not changed and there is nothing indicating her aversion. So if her husband gave her choice while she was sitting and she stood, her choice is invalidated because standing is evidence of aversion. [4] His saying: and disliked... etc.; the aspect of dislike is that it involves abandoning some of the Quran and fleeing from the prostration, and that is not from the character of the believers, and also it involves changing the composition of the Quran and breaking its order. And apparently the dislike is tahrimi (prohibitive), as is in al-Nahr. [1] His saying: to repel; to repel the suspicion of preference since all of it as the speech of Allah is not in one rank, even though some have extra virtue in another consideration. (2) His saying: lest it become obligatory; he said in al-Binayah: because the listener perhaps does not perform it at the time due to a preventer, and does not perform it afterward due to forgetfulness, so the obligation remains upon him. (1) al-Binayah (2:736).
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addresses several rulings related to the prostration of recitation (sajdah al-tilawah). The passage begins by stating a key ruling: it is disliked (makruh) to intentionally skip a prostration verse (i.e., to omit reciting it) and to recite the rest of the surah without prostrating. However, the opposite—merely reciting the prostration verse without the rest—is not disliked. The commentary explains that the dislike for skipping the verse is because it involves abandoning part of the Quran and escaping from the prostration, which is contrary to the character of believers. It also changes the order of the Quran. The text notes that this dislike is apparently tahrimi (prohibitive), meaning it is close to haram. As for the opposite (reciting only the prostration verse), it is not disliked because it shows eagerness to obey. The passage then continues with a discussion on the definition of 'place' for multiple prostrations: corners of a house or mosque are treated as one location for the rule that moving does not require repeating the prostration, while branches of a single tree are considered separate places in the apparent narration (but one place in al-Nawadir). This is followed by an explanation that standing does not change the session (majlis) for the purposes of repetition of prostration, contrary to the case of a woman given the choice of divorce (mukhayyarah), where standing indicates aversion and invalidates her choice. The latter part of the passage recommends (mandub) that when reciting a prostration verse, one should join one or two verses before it to it. This is to avoid any suspicion of preferring some Quranic verses over others in terms of recitation order. Additionally, it is considered mustahsan (preferable) to conceal the recitation of the prostration verse from a listener if the listener may not be in a state of wudu, lest the prostration become obligatory upon him and he might fail to perform it, leaving an obligatory act pending. The commentary cites al-Binayah for this reasoning. Overall, the passage provides practical guidance for the etiquette and legal rulings surrounding the recitation of prostration verses.

Key Terms
سجود التلاوة (sajdah al-tilawah): The prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a specific verse of the Quran (ayat al-sajdah).
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrimiyyah): Prohibitive dislike; an act that is close to haram and is sinful but without the definitive evidence of prohibition.
استحسان (istihsan): Preference; a juristic method of preferring a stronger evidence or public interest over a weak analogy.
مخيرة (mukhayyarah): A woman whose husband gives her the choice to divorce herself. Used here as an analogy to explain that standing changes the session in divorce context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on skipping a prostration verse and reciting the rest of the surah without prostrating?
A) It is recommended (mandub).
B) It is disliked with prohibitive dislike (makruh tahrimi).
C) It is permissible (mubah).
D) It is obligatory (wajib).

Key Takeaway
Skipping a prostration verse is makruh tahrimi; one should recite it and prostrate or at least not intentionally omit it.

Attaching Verses and Concealing Supplication in Salah
Ruling on attaching verses and concealing supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attaching Quranic verses before supplication in salah and explain the reasoning for concealing the supplication from the listener.

Core Idea
Attaching verses before supplication prevents misunderstanding of preference; concealing it avoids imposing obligation on the listener.

This passage from al-Wiqayah, with commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, addresses two etiquettes of supplication (du'a) within salah. The first is a recommendation to precede the du'a with a verse or two of the Quran, and the second is a preference to keep the du'a quiet so that others do not hear it. The reasons given highlight the Hanafi concern for both the sanctity of the Quran and the avoidance of placing unnecessary burdens on the congregation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The text states two things: (1) 'nudiba damm aya...' (it is recommended to attach a verse or two) and (2) 'istuhsina ikhfauha 'an al-sami'' (it is considered good to conceal it from the listener). Note the different legal terminology: 'nudiba' (recommended) and 'istuhsina' (juristic preference).
The first is a sunnah act; the second is based on istihsan to avoid potential harm.
2
Understand the reason for attaching verses
The reason given is 'daf'an li-tawahhumi al-tafdil' (to prevent the misunderstanding of preference). The commentary explains that all Quranic verses are of equal rank as the speech of Allah, and attaching them to the supplication shows that the du'a is not being preferred over the Quran.
The concern is that if one makes du'a without preceding Quranic verses, someone might think the du'a is more virtuous than Quran recitation.
3
Understand the reason for concealing the supplication
The reason is 'li'alla tajiba 'ala al-sami'' (so that it does not become obligatory upon the listener). The commentary from al-Binaya explains that if the listener hears the du'a, it might become wajib on him to also make that du'a, but he may not be able to do it immediately (e.g., lacking wudu) or may forget later, thus leaving an obligation unfulfilled.
This shows Hanafi sensitivity to potential over-obligation.
4
Note the expansion in the commentary
The commentary expands the second ruling by citing al-Binaya, which provides the reasoning about the listener's potential excuse and forgetfulness. This is an example of 'expansion probe' where the commentator clarifies the 'illa (effective cause).
The 'illa is the avoidance of imposing an unwanted duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُدِبَ ضَمَّ آية، أو آيتين قبلها إليها )؛ دفعاً لتوهم التفضيل. واسْتَحْسِنَ اخفاؤُها عن السامع) ؛ لئلا تجب على السامع، فإنه ربما يكون السامع غير متوضئ [١] قوله : دفعاً؛ لتوهم التفضيل إذ الكل من حيث أنه كلام الله لا في رتبة واحدة، وإن كان لبعضها فضل زائد باعتبار آخر. (۲) قوله : لئلا تجب؛ قال في «البناية»): لأن السامع ربما لا يؤديها في الحال المانع، ولا يؤديها بعد ذلك بسبب النسيان، فيبقى عليه الواجب.
And it is recommended to attach a verse or two before it (the supplication) to prevent the misunderstanding of (the supplication being) preferred. And it is considered good to conceal it from the listener so that it does not become obligatory upon the listener, for perhaps the listener is not in a state of wudu. [1] His saying: 'to prevent the misunderstanding of preference' because all (parts of the Quran) are not at the same rank from the aspect that it is the speech of Allah, although some have extra virtue by another consideration. (2) His saying: 'so that it does not become obligatory'; he said in al-Binaya: because the listener perhaps might not perform it immediately due to a valid excuse, and does not perform it afterwards due to forgetfulness, so the obligatory remains upon him.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling regarding supplication (du'a) in salah. The text of al-Wiqayah states that it is recommended (nudiba) to attach a verse or two before the supplication. The reason given is to prevent the misunderstanding that the supplication is being given preference over the Quran, since all Quranic verses are the speech of Allah. The commentator adds that it is considered good (istahsana) to conceal the supplication from the listener, because if the listener hears it, it might become obligatory (wajib) upon him to also make the supplication, but he may not be able to do it immediately (e.g., lacking wudu) or may forget later, leaving a duty unfulfilled. This reasoning is from al-Binaya. The passage highlights a fiqhi subtlety: that the listener is not obligated to repeat the supplication unless he intends it, but to avoid potential difficulty, it is better to conceal it.

Key Terms
نُدِبَ (nudiba): It is recommended; a ruling of mandub (commendable) that is not obligatory.
اسْتَحْسِنَ (istuhsina): It is considered good; a ruling based on istihsan (juristic preference).
السامع (al-sami‘): The listener who hears the supplication being recited in salah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Attaching a verse before du'a and concealing it are recommended to protect Quran's status and avoid burdening others with potential obligations.

Definition of a Traveler for Legal Dispensations
Definition of a traveler: intention, distance, and departure from residence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions that define a traveler in Hanafi fiqh and explain why each is necessary for legal dispensations.

Core Idea
A traveler is not merely one who leaves home; he must intend a journey of at least three days and actually depart from his town.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of a traveler (musafir). Understanding this definition is crucial because it determines who qualifies for rukhsas such as shortening prayer (qasr) and combining prayers. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the etymology and the two essential conditions: intention (qasd) and distance (three days). We will also see how the hadith on wiping over socks provides the basis for the three-day standard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal conditions for travel
From the text: 'what is considered in travel are two things: determination to travel and leaving the town.' Note that these two are both necessary. Without either, the person remains a resident. The author also mentions that passing by houses without intention or with intention for less than three days does not count.
2
Analyze the condition of intention (qasd)
The text states: 'if he passes by the houses of the village without intending to travel, it is not travel.' Intention (niyyah) is thus a prerequisite. This is consistent with the general principle that acts require intention.
3
Analyze the condition of distance (three days and nights)
The hadith on wiping over socks mentions 'three days and their nights' for a traveler. The Hanafis derive the minimum travel distance from this. The lām in 'al-musafir' is interpreted as for the genus or for generality, implying that the ruling applies to all travelers, and if the minimum were less, the specification would be meaningless.
This is a dalil from Sunnah, albeit ahad, with zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Confirm the leaving of residence
The third implied condition is that the traveler must have left the built-up area (biyut) of his town. The text says 'and he has left the houses of his town.' If he is still within the town or its suburbs, he is not considered traveling.
This condition is explicit in the last line: 'Fariq biyut baladih'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المسافر [١] قوله : صلاة المسافر؛ أي هذا باب في أحكام صلاة المسافر من إضافة الشيء إلى فاعله، والسفر بالفتح: الكشف، وإنَّما سُمّي السَّفَر بفتحتين سَفَراً؛ لأنَّه يكشفُ عن أخلاق الرجال، وأنَّه ينكشف للأرض وتنكشف هي له. [٢] قوله : هو؛ الإشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بالمسافر عنوان الباب مطلق المسافر، بل المسافر الخاص الذي أديرت عليه الأحكام. (۳) قوله : قصد...الخ؛ المعتبر في السفر أمران أحدهما: عزم السير، وثانيهما: الخروج من البلد، فإن جاوز بيوت المصرِ غير قاصد للسفر لا يكون سفراً، وإن جاوزها قاصداً مدة ما دون السفر لا يكون سفراً. كذا في «البناية). [٤] قوله : ثلاثة أيام ولياليها؛ الأصلُ في هذا التقدير حديث المسح على الخفين، وهو: «يمسح المسافر ثلاثة أيام ولياليها)، على ما مرَّ في بابه، فإِنَّهُ يدلُّ على أنَّ أقل مدة السفرِ هذا القدر، فإن اللام في المسافر للجنس أو للاستغراق، ولو كان السفر المعتبر أقل لما كان لهذا التَّعميم معنى. [0] قوله : وفارق بيوت بلده؛ أي موضع إقامته، إن لم يكن بلداً بل قرية، أو كان بلداً غير بلده الذي هو موطنه
Chapter on the traveler's prayer. [1] His saying: 'the traveler's prayer' i.e., this is a chapter on the rulings of the traveler's prayer, as an idafa of a thing to its doer. And safar (with fatha) means uncovering. It is called safar (with two fathas) because it uncovers the character of men, and because it becomes uncovered to the land and the land becomes uncovered to him. [2] His saying: 'he' — indicating that what is meant by 'traveler' is not the absolute traveler of the chapter title, but the specific traveler upon whom the rulings revolve. (3) His saying: 'intends... etc.' — what is considered in travel are two things: one is the determination to travel, and the second is leaving the town. So if he passes by the houses of the village without intending to travel, it is not travel, and if he passes them intending a duration less than travel, it is not travel. Thus in al-Binayah. [4] His saying: 'three days and their nights' — the basis of this estimation is the hadith of wiping over the socks: 'The traveler wipes for three days and their nights' as has passed in its chapter, for it indicates that the minimum period of travel is this amount. For the lām in 'al-musafir' is for the genus or for generality. And if the considered travel were less, this generalization would have no meaning. [0] His saying: 'and he has left the houses of his town' i.e., his place of residence, if it is not a town but a village, or if it is a town other than his town which is his homeland.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the traveler's prayer by defining who is legally a traveler. The commentary begins by explaining the word 'safar' etymologically: it means uncovering because travel reveals people's true character. The key definition: a traveler is someone who (1) intends to travel, (2) for a distance equivalent to three days and nights of normal walking, and (3) has left the built-up area of his town. The distance is derived from the hadith on wiping over socks which specifies three days for a traveler, implying that anything less is not considered travel for legal dispensations. The commentary also clarifies that merely passing through without intention or traveling for a shorter duration does not constitute travel in the legal sense.

Key Terms
المسافر (al-musāfir): A person who meets two conditions: intention to travel and leaving the dwellings of his town for a distance of three days/normal journey.
السفر (al-safar): The act of traveling; literally 'uncovering' because it reveals character.
قصد (qaṣd): Intention or determination to travel; without niyyah, no legal travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of a traveler requires intention, a minimum journey of three days, and actual departure from town; missing any of these keeps the person a resident.

Definition of the Traveler in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines the legal traveler for prayer concessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the legal traveler (musafir) in Hanafi fiqh by stating its two essential conditions (intention and departure) and the required minimum distance, and identify the dalil from the hadith of wiping over khuffs.

Core Idea
A traveler in Hanafi fiqh is one who intends a three-day journey and has left his town.

The concept of safar (travel) is central to several prayer concessions. But who exactly is a traveler? The Hanafi school does not leave this vague; it provides a precise legal definition grounded in the hadith of wiping over khuffs. In this lesson, we dissect the definition and its components as explained by the commentator on al-Wiqayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the definition
The text clarifies that 'traveler' here is not any traveler, but the specific one for whom rulings are enacted. Focus on the precise legal meaning.
The definition is operative for determining entitlement to concessions like qasr.
2
Distill the two conditions
Extract the two essential conditions: (1) the resolve (cazm) to travel, and (2) physically leaving the dwellings of one's town.
Both conditions must be present; missing either negates traveler status.
3
Trace the evidence for three days
Connect the three-day minimum to the hadith on khuffs. Note the linguistic reasoning: the definite article 'al-' in 'al-musafir' indicates generality, implying the concession applies to all travelers, thus the minimum travel period is three days.
This is the primary dalil; other schools may use different measures.
4
Understand the measurement of distance
Recognize that 'three days' is measured by average travel pace (sīr wasaṭ), which varies by terrain: land (camel/walking), sea (moderate wind), mountains (appropriate pace).
This standardizes the distance across different modes of travel.
5
Appreciate the linguistic insight
Note that the word 'safar' means uncovering, because travel reveals character and exposes the traveler to new lands.
This is a beneficial observation but does not affect the legal ruling.

Source Text

Chapter on the prayer of the traveler. He is one who intends an average journey of three days and their nights, and separates from the dwellings of his town. His saying: 'Prayer of the traveler' i.e., this chapter is on the rulings of the traveler's prayer, from adding a thing to its doer. Travel (safar) by fatḥ is uncovering; it is called safar with two fatḥas because it uncovers the character of men, and he is uncovered to the land and it is uncovered to him. His saying: 'He' indicates that what is meant by 'traveler' in the chapter heading is not any traveler, but the specific traveler for whom rulings are enacted. His saying: 'and separates from the dwellings of his town' i.e., his place of residence, whether it is not a town but a village, or a town other than his hometown. And for the average (speed) on land is the travel of camels and a pedestrian, for sea is moderate wind, and for mountains is what befits it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the traveler (musāfir) in Hanafi fiqh, establishing the conditions under which a person becomes a traveler eligible for concessions such as shortening prayer. The author clarifies that 'traveler' in this context is not any person who has traveled, but one who meets specific criteria. The definition includes two essential conditions: (1) an intentional resolution to travel, and (2) physically leaving the dwellings of one's town. The minimum distance for travel is set as a journey of three days and nights, based on the hadith about wiping over khuffs. The speed of travel (sīr wasaṭ) is defined according to the mode: on land by camel or walking pace, at sea by a moderate wind, and in mountains by what is appropriate. The commentary from the sharh explains that the term 'safar' linguistically means uncovering, because travel reveals people's true character. It also stresses that the traveler must have the genuine intention to undertake a journey of at least three days; a shorter journey does not confer traveler status. The proof for the three-day duration is the well-known hadith that grants the wiping concession for three days to a traveler, implying that this is the minimum period for travel.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Legally: traveling a distance of three days and nights (at moderate pace) with intention to leave one's dwelling.
مسافر (musāfir): One who intends a three-day journey and has left behind the dwellings of his place of residence.
السير الوسط (al-sayr al-wasaṭ): Average travel speed; for land: camel or walking pace; for sea: moderate wind; for mountains: appropriate pace.

Concessions for the Traveler Including the Sinful Traveler
States that travel concessions remain even for a sinful traveler until return or 15-day stay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on travel concessions for a sinful traveler and explain the proof for the fifteen-day residency period.

Core Idea
Travel concessions are based on the fact of travel, not the traveler's piety — even a highway robber may shorten prayer.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya clarifies that the concessions of travel — shortening prayer and breaking fast — apply to every traveler, regardless of their intent. The author refutes the Shāfiʿī position that a sinner does not deserve the blessing. It also specifies that the travel status ends when one intends to stay fifteen days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The author states that travel concessions (shortening prayer and breaking fast) last until the traveler returns home or intends to stay fifteen days. This ruling applies even to a sinful traveler.
2
Locate the dalil for the fifteen-day rule
The proof is the hadith of Ibn ʿAbbās: 'When you arrive at a town and intend to stay fifteen days, complete the prayer.' This hadith is cited to define the end of travel status.
The hadith is recorded by al-Ṭaḥāwī and Ibn Abī Shayba.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'akmil al-ṣalāh' (complete the prayer) indicates that the concession of qasr ends when one intends fifteen days. The number fifteen is explicitly mentioned, making it binding.
The wāw in 'wa-in kāna' is original, linking to the earlier phrase.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on sinful traveler
Imam al-Shāfiʿī holds that a sinful traveler does not receive concessions because they are blessings from Allah. The Hanafi school refutes this.
The author explicitly says 'to refute al-Shāfiʿī'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is that the concession is absolute (rukhṣa muṭlaqa) and tied to travel itself (tunāṭu bi-l-safar min ḥaythu huwa safar). Sin is extraneous. This is the muftā bihī position.
All three eponyms (Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad) agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله رخص تدوم : كالقصر في الصلاة والإفطار في الصوم، وإن كان عاصياً في سفره حتى يدخل بلده، حتى يدخل متعلّق بقوله تدوم، أو ينوي إقامة نصف شهر ببلدة، أو قرية، منها : أي من الرخص . قوله : كالقصر ... الخ ؛ الفرق بين رخصةِ الصَّلاةِ ورخصة الصيام أنَّ الصيام رُخص فيه المسافرُ إن شاءَ صام وإن شاء لم يصم، إلا أنَّ الصَّومَ من قوي عليه أفضل، وقصرُ الصَّلاةِ عندنا رخصة إسقاط فلو أتم أساء كما سيجيء. قوله : وإن كان الواو أصلية، يعني وإن كان المسافر عاصياً في سفره ؛ كما إذا سافر لقطع الطريق أو لإيذاء المسلمين ومحاربتهم ، صرح به نفياً لقول الشافعي لله فإنَّه يقول : الرخصة نعمةٌ فلا ينالها العاصي، ونحن نقول : الرخصة مطلقة، والرخصة إنَّما تناط بالسفرِ من حيث أنه سفر، والعصيان فيه أمر زائد. قوله: نصف شهر ؛ أي خمسة عشر يوماً مع لياليها، لقول ابن عباس له : «إذا قدمت بلدة وأنت مسافر، وفي نفسك أن تقوم خمسة عشر يوماً فأكمل الصلاة»، أخرجه الطَّحَاوِيُّ في «شرح معاني «الآثار»، وروى ابن أبي شيبة نحوه عن ابن عمر.
And he has concessions that last: like shortening the prayer and breaking the fast in fasting, even if he is sinful in his travel until he enters his town — 'until he enters' is attached to His saying 'last' — or intends to reside for half a month in a town or a village — that is, from the concessions. His saying: 'like shortening... etc.' The difference between the concession of prayer and the concession of fasting is that fasting is a concession for the traveler: if he wishes he fasts and if he wishes he does not fast, except that fasting is better for the one who is strong. Shortening prayer with us is a concession of omission; if he completes (the full prayer) he does wrong, as will come. His saying: 'and if he is' — the wāw is original — meaning if the traveler is sinful in his travel, such as if he travels to cut the road (highway robbery) or to harm Muslims and fight them. He explicitly stated it to refute the saying of al-Shafiʿī — may Allah (have mercy on him) — for he says: the concession is a blessing, so the sinner does not attain it. We say: the concession is absolute, and the concession is attached to travel insofar as it is travel, and sin is an additional matter. His saying: 'half a month' i.e., fifteen days with their nights, based on the saying of Ibn ʿAbbās to him: 'When you arrive at a town as a traveler, and you intend to stay fifteen days, then complete the prayer.' Recorded by al-Ṭaḥāwī in Sharḥ Maʿānī al-Āthār, and Ibn Abī Shayba transmitted something similar from Ibn ʿUmar.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage deals with the duration and scope of the travel concessions (rukhṣaṣ) in Hanafi fiqh. The author states that the concessions of shortening prayer and breaking the fast continue throughout the journey until the traveler either returns to his hometown or intends to stay in a place for fifteen days (including nights). The key point is that these concessions apply even to a traveler who is sinful (ʿāṣ) in his journey, such as a highway robber or someone traveling to harm Muslims. This is in direct opposition to Imam al-Shāfiʿī, who held that a sinner does not deserve the blessing of the concession. The Hanafi argument is that the concession is linked to the fact of travel itself, not to the moral character of the traveler; sin is an extraneous factor. The passage also distinguishes between the concession in prayer (which is a concession of omission — it is improper to complete the prayer) and the concession in fasting (which is a choice, but fasting is better for those who can bear it). The proof for the fifteen-day period is the hadith from Ibn ʿAbbās that one should complete the prayer when intending to stay fifteen days. Thus, the travel status (and its concessions) ends when one forms the intention to stay half a month.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhṣa): A legal concession that permits an act normally restricted or omits an obligatory act due to a valid excuse (here: travel).
عاص (ʿāṣ): One who is disobeying Allah by engaging in sinful conduct during travel (e.g., highway robbery).
إقامة (iqāma): Settling in a place; when a traveler intends to stay for fifteen days, the travel status ends and concessions cease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding a traveler who is traveling to commit highway robbery (cutting the road)?
A) He does not get any concessions because he is sinful
B) He gets concessions only if he repents before starting the journey
C) He still gets the concessions of shortening prayer and breaking fast
D) He must avoid all concessions as a form of repentance

Key Takeaway
Travel concessions are attached to the fact of travel, not the traveler's moral state, and last until returning home or intending to stay fifteen days.

Travel Concessions for the Sinful Traveler
Clarifying the definition of middle pace and the persistence of travel rukhsas for sinful travelers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on travel concessions for sinners, explain the Hanafi proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Travel concessions are based on the journey itself, not the traveler's piety—a bandit may shorten prayer.

When a person travels, even for a sinful purpose, the Hanafi school holds that he may still shorten his prayers and break his fast. This seems counterintuitive: why give a blessing to a sinner? The commentary explains the reasoning and refutes the Shafi'i objection. In this lesson, you will dissect the proof and understand the principle of 'rukhsa mutlaqa.'

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: travel concessions (qasr, iftar) continue for any traveler, even one who sins during the journey.
The sin referred to is a major sin like highway robbery.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that a sinful traveler does not get the concession because it is a blessing and blessings are not for sinners. Find his dalil in the passage.
The Shafi'i proof is not quoted verbatim, but summarized.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the cause (safar) and an accidental attribute (sin). The concession is attached to travel as such, not to the traveler's moral state.
The phrase 'al-rukhsa mutlaqa' (absolute concession) is key.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a bandit may pray qasr and break fast; in the Shafi'i school, he must pray full and fast.
This affects real-life fatwa.
5
Note the definition of middle pace
The passage also defines the 'middle pace' for travel (land: camel/pedestrian; sea: moderate wind; mountains: suitable pace). This is the benchmark for travel speed that triggers concessions.
This ensures the travel is genuine and not too slow/fast.

And the middle pace is considered: for land (travel) the pace of camels and pedestrians, for sea the moderation of wind, and for mountains what is suitable for it. And he has concessions that continue even if he is disobedient in his travel until he enters his town or intends to stay half a month in a town or village, from among them. And consider for land the pace of camels and pedestrians, and for sea the moderation of wind, and for mountains what is suitable for it. And he has concessions that continue, such as shortening in prayer and breaking fast in fasting, even if he is disobedient in his travel until he enters his town; 'until he enters' is attached to 'continue', (or intends to stay half a month in a town or village from among them): i.e., from the concessions. [1] His saying: 'for land... etc.' means: travel if in the desert, then the middle pace in it is the pace of camels and the pedestrian, i.e., the one walking on his feet; and if at sea, then the considered pace is when the wind by which the ship sails is moderate, neither stormy nor calm; and if in the mountains, then the considered pace is according to what is suitable for them. [2] His saying: 'such as shortening... etc.'; the difference between the concession of prayer and the concession of fasting is that fasting is a concession in which the traveler may fast or not fast, except that fasting is better for the one who is strong, while shortening prayer according to us is a concession of omission; if he completes it, he does wrong, as will come. [3] His saying: 'even if' – the letter waw is original, meaning even if the traveler is disobedient in his travel, such as when he travels for highway robbery or to harm Muslims and fight them. He stated it to negate the saying of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he says: the concession is a blessing, so the disobedient does not attain it. And we say: the concession is absolute, and the concession is attached to travel inasmuch as it is travel, and disobedience in it is an additional matter. [4] His saying: 'half a month' i.e., fifteen days with their nights, based on the saying of Ibn Abbas to him: 'When you arrive at a town while you are a traveler, and you intend to stay fifteen days, then complete the prayer.' Narrated by al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, and Ibn Abi Shayba narrated something similar from Ibn Umar: (1) Indeed, from Mujahid, he said: 'Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, used to complete the prayer when he resolved to stay fifteen days' in I'la al-Sunan (7:297): narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba with a sound chain, and Sunan al-Tirmidhi (2:431), etc.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the commentary on the matn. The author explains the definition of the 'middle pace' for travel on land, sea, and mountains. Then he addresses the ruling that travel concessions (rukhsas) like shortening prayer and breaking fast continue for a traveler even if he is disobedient (e.g., a bandit). The commentator contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, which holds that a sinner cannot benefit from the concession because it is a blessing. The Hanafi response is that the concession is tied to the act of travel itself, not the moral state of the traveler; sin is an extra factor that does not negate the license. The passage also clarifies the minimum period of residence (fifteen days) that would abrogate the travel concessions, citing hadith from Ibn Abbas and the practice of Ibn Umar.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsa): A legal dispensation or concession that relaxes the standard obligation due to a valid excuse (travel).
عاصٍ ('asi): A person committing sin; here specifically one who travels to commit sin (e.g., highway robbery).
نصف شهر (nisf shahr): Half a month, defined as fifteen days including nights, which is the maximum period of travel that still permits concessions if one intends less.

In the Hanafi school, travel concessions are attached to the journey itself, not the traveler's piety; even a sinful traveler may shorten prayer and break fast.

When Must a Traveler Shorten Prayer?
Listing conditions for obligatory shortening with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions under which the Hanafi school obliges shortening the four-rakat prayers and apply them to realistic travel scenarios.

Core Idea
Shortening is obligatory unless you intend fifteen days in one place.

The four-rakat obligatory prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Isha) are shortened to two rakats for a traveler. But when exactly does this obligation apply? The Hanafi school specifies four scenarios, each with a clear ruling and proof. This lesson unpacks those conditions and the evidence behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is shortening (qasr) the four-rakat fard prayers. In the Hanafi school, this is wajib (obligatory) when the travel conditions are met, not merely permissible.
Maghrib (3 rakat) and Fajr (2 rakat) are never shortened.
2
List the four conditions for wajib
The text gives four scenarios: (1) intending less than half a month (15 days) in one place; (2) intending the full half-month but splitting it between two or more places; (3) entering a town intending to leave within a day or two, even if the stay becomes long; (4) an army entering enemy territory or besieging a fort, regardless of intention.
Half a month = 15 days. This is the threshold for becoming a resident.
3
Understand the 'two places' nuance
If a traveler intends to stay a total of 15 days but splits them (e.g., 10 days in one city and 5 in another), he is not considered a resident and must shorten. The Prophet did this during Hajjat al-Wada, staying in multiple locations without completing.
The proof is the Prophet's continuous shortening despite multi-day stays.
4
Recognize the exceptional army case
An army in dar al-harb (enemy territory) always shortens, even if it intends a long stay, because the situation is uncertain: they may flee or conquer. Their intention does not take effect, unlike a single Muslim entering with aman (safe conduct).
This is a special Hanafi ruling based on the uncertainty of residence.
5
Connect the proofs
The text cites hadith: the Prophet's practice during Hajjat al-Wada, Ibn Umar staying six months in Azerbaijan shortening, and the army tradition. These show that intention of residence (15 days) is the key, not duration alone.
The proofs are ahad (single-transmitter) but sufficient for Hanafi obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He shortens his four-rakat obligatory prayer. So he shortens if he intends less than half a month, or intends its duration in two places, or enters a town intending to leave tomorrow or the day after, and his stay becomes long; and likewise an army that enters enemy territory or besieges a fort there. [1] His saying: 'his four-rakat obligatory prayer' excludes the three-rakat like Maghrib and the two-rakat like Fajr. [2] His saying: 'in two places' – such that he intends in one place ten days, for example, and in another place the remainder; thus he is not considered a resident. Indeed, it is established in the authentic books that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) shortened during the journey of the Farewell Pilgrimage until he returned to Madinah, even though he stayed in Makkah, Mina, Arafat and elsewhere for ten days. [3] His saying: 'or enters a town, etc.' – meaning he enters a town and intends to leave in a day or two, and does not intend to stay fifteen days, and his stay there becomes long with this intention.
