[4] His saying: 'and likewise' – i.e., an army shortens when it enters dar al-harb for raiding, or besieges a fort in dar al-harb, even if they intend to stay half a month, because they are between fleeing if they are defeated and staying if they conquer; thus that place is not their place of residence, so their intention does not take effect, just as it does not take effect in the desert. This is contrary to the case when a single Muslim enters their land with aman, for he completes if he intends residence for its duration. (1) From Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him: that he stayed in Azerbaijan for six months shortening prayer, and he said: 'If I resolve to stay, I complete' in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (2:533); and from Jabir ibn Abd Allah, may Allah be pleased with him: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, stayed in Tabuk for twenty days shortening prayer' in Sunan Abi Dawud (1:393) and Musnad Ahmad (3: was authenticated by our shaykh al-Arna'ut) and Sahih Ibn Hibban (6:456); and in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad (1:298): narrated from al-Hasan: We were with al-Hasan ibn Samurah in some parts of Persia for two years, and he did not combine [prayers] nor increase beyond two rakats. And it is narrated that Anas ibn Malik stayed in Shaam for two months with Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan, praying two rakats. And there are other reports mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Rayah.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the conditions under which a traveler shortens the four-rakat obligatory prayers (excluding Maghrib and Fajr). The matn states four scenarios where qasr applies: (1) intending less than half a month (fifteen days); (2) intending the full half-month but dividing it between two locations; (3) entering a town with the intention to leave in a day or two, even if the stay becomes prolonged; (4) an army entering enemy territory or besieging a fort there. The commentary clarifies each: the first scenario is clear; the second is exemplified by the Prophet's practice during Hajjat al-Wada where he stayed in multiple places but only for a total of ten days; the third is proven by Ibn Umar's six-month stay in Azerbaijan while still shortening prayer because he never intended fifteen days; and the fourth scenario is specific to an army which, unlike a single Muslim entering with aman, is not considered a resident due to the uncertainty of staying or fleeing. The proofs include hadith from the Prophet's practice and reports from the Companions.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rakat fard prayers to two rakats for a traveler.
نصف شهر (nisf shahr): Half a month, defined as fifteen days, which is the threshold for becoming a resident.
عسكر ('askar): An army that enters enemy territory; its intention of residence does not affect qasr due to uncertainty of staying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The key to qasr in the Hanafi school is the intention of residence for half a month or more in one location, not the actual duration of stay.

Conditions for Qasr: Intention of Residence
Legal explanation of when qasr is replaced by tamam due to intention of residence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a traveler must pray complete prayers due to intention of residence and identify exceptions for soldiers and tent-dwellers.

Core Idea
A traveler shortens prayer unless he intends residence of half a month (15 days).

Traveling changes the obligation of prayer: the four-rak'ah prayers are shortened to two. But when does a traveler become a resident and must pray full prayers? The key is the intention to stay (niyyat al-iqamah). This lesson unpacks the conditions and exceptions from the Hanafi text, including the nuanced case of tent-dwellers and soldiers in war zones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its proof
State the default: a traveler shortens (qasr) all four-rak'ah obligatory prayers. The proof comes from the Prophet's practice and the Companions' actions, as cited in the text.
The text uses the phrase 'qasr fardihi al-ruba'i' – notice the distinction from three-rak'ah (Maghrib) and two-rak'ah (Fajr).
2
Define the threshold for residence (iqamah)
The halting point is intention to stay for half a month (15 days). If the intention is less, the traveler shortens. If the intention is 15 days or more, he becomes a muqim and must complete his prayers.
Hanafis set 15 days; Shafi'is set 4 days. Know the difference.
3
Analyze the exceptions: two locations, unsettled intention, soldiers, rebels
If a traveler intends 15 days total but splits it between two locations, he does not become a muqim. If he enters a city with no firm intention to stay 15 days, even if he stays longer, he remains a musafir. Soldiers in dar al-harb or besieging a fort, and troops fighting rebels (ahl al-baghy) also shorten, as their situation is unstable.
The text explicitly connects these rulings to the idea that 'the intention does not take effect' due to uncertainty.
4
Examine the special case of tent-dwellers (ahl al-akhbiyah)
Bedouins and nomadic tent-dwellers: the most correct Hanafi view is that if they intend half a month in their tents, they become residents and must complete prayers. This is because for them, staying is the norm and travel is the exception.
This resolves a later intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: some said they never become residents, but the mufta bihi is that they do.
5
Evaluate the evidence and the tarjih
The primary evidence includes the hadith of Jabir (Prophet staying 20 days in Tabuk shortening) and Ibn Umar's six-month stay in Azerbaijan. The Hanafis set 15 days based on general practice and analogy. The later scholars' preference for the ahl al-akhbiyah view is cited from al-Kifayah and al-Mabsut.
The proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala, affecting the hukm level (wajib, not fard).

Source Text

كتاب الصلاة صلاة مسافر قصر فرضِهِ الرباعي ، فيقصرُ إِن نَوَى أقل من نصف شهر، أو نوى مدتها بموضعين، أو دخل بلداً عازماً خروجه غداً ، أو بعد غدٍ وطال مكته ، وكذا عسكر دَخَلَ أرض حرب، أو حاصر حصناً فيها (قصر فرضِهِ الرباعي ، فيقصر إن نَوَى أقل من نصف شهر، أو نوى مدتها) : أي مدة الإقامة ، وهي نصف شهر، (بموضعين ، أو دخل بلدا عازماً خروجه غداً، أو بعد غدٍ وطال مكنه ، وكذا عسكرٌ دَخَلَ أرض حرب، أو حاصر حصناً فيها [1] قوله : فرضه الرباعي ؛ احتراز عن الثلاثي كالمغرب، والثنائي كالفجر. [٢] قوله : بموضعَين ؛ بأن ينوي في موضع عشرة أيام مثلاً ، وفي موضع آخر ما بقي فإنَّه لا يعد مقيماً ، فقد ثبت في كتب الصحاح عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كان يقصر في سفرِ حجة الوداع إلى أن رجع إلى المدينة، مع أنه أقام بمكة ومنى وعرفات وغيرها عشراً. (۳) قوله : أو دخل بلدا... الخ ؛ يعني دخل بلداً وقصد أن يخرج في يوم أو يومين ، ولم ينو إقامة خمسة عشر يوماً ، وطالت إقامته هناك على هذا القصد، وقد ثبت عن ابن عمر أنه أقام بأذربيجان ستَّة أشهر ولم يتمّ الصَّلاة، أخرجه عبد الرزاق في «مصنفه»، والبَيْهَقِيُّ في كتاب «المعرفة»، ومثله عن جمع من الصحابة في «صحيح مسلم»، و«سنن البيهقي» وغيرهما. [٤] قوله : وكذا ؛ أي يقصر عسكر دخل دار حرب للغزو، أو حاصروا حصناً بالكسر في دار الحرب، وإن قصدوا إقامة نصف شهر ؛ لأنهم بين أن يفروا إذا غُلبوا، وبين أن يقروا إن غلبوا ، فلم يكن ذلك الموضع موضع إقامتهم، فلا تعمل فيه نيتهم، كما لا تعمل في الصحراء، بخلاف ما إذا دخل واحدٌ من المسلمين في دارهم بأمان، فإنّه يتم إذا قصد الإقامة مدتها. ،(۲۹۵ (۱) فعن ابن عمر الله : أنه أقام بأذربيجان ستة أشهر يقصر الصلاة وكان قال : إذا أزمعت إقامة أتم» في «مصنف عبد الرزاق (۲) : ٥۳۳) ، وعن جابر بن عبد الله له : «أقام رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بتبوك عشرين يوماً يقصر الصلاة» في «سنن أبي داود (۱ : ۳۹۳)، و(مسند أحمد»(۳: وصححه شيخنا الأرنؤوط، وصحيح ابن حبان (٦ : ٤٥٦»، وفي «التعليق الممجد» (۱ : ۲۹۸) : وروي عن الحسن : كنا مع الحسن بن سمرة ببعض بلاد فارس سنتين فكان لا يجمع ولا يزيد على ركعتين، وروي أن أنس بن مالك أقام بالشام شهرين مع عبد الملك بن مروان يصلي ركعتين، وفي الباب آثار أخر ذكرها الزيلعي «نصب الراية». ». أو أهلَ البَغْي في دارنا في غير مصر، وإن نووا إقامة مُدَّتِها لا أهل أخْبيَةٍ نووها في الأصح أو أهلَ البَغْي" في دارنا في غير مصر ، وإن نووا إقامة مدتها) : أي يقصر الجماعة المذكورو وإن نووا إقامة نصف شهر ؛ لأنهم لم يصيروا مقيمين بنية الإقامة. (لا أهل أخبية " نووها في الأصح) : أي لا يقصر أهل أَخْبيَةٍ نووا إقامة نصف شهر في أَخْبيَتِهم ؛ لأن نية الإقامة تصح منهم في الصحراء ؛ لأن الإقامة أصل لا تبطل بانتقالهم من مَرْعَى إلى مَرْعَى، هذا هو الصحيح. . [۳] [١] قوله : أو أهل البغي ؛ أي حاصر عسكر المسلمين أهل البغي ـ وهو من خرج عن طاعة السلطان في دار الإسلام . فإنّهم يقصرون ؛ لأنهم بين القرار والفرار، وهذه المسألة وإن كان يُعلم حكمُها مما سبق لكن ذكرها لدفع توهم أن نية الإقامة في دارِ الحرب إنَّما لم تصح ؛ لأنها منقطعة عن دار الإسلام، فكانت كالمفازة، بخلاف مدينة أهل البغي ؛ لأنها دار الإسلام. فأشار إلى أنه لا يصح نيَّةُ الإقامة ممن يُحاصرهم في دار الإسلام أيضاً، وبهذا يعلم أن تقييد المسألة بغيرِ مصر اتفاقي ، فإنَّ الحكم في المحاصرة بمصر أيضاً كذلك ؛ للوجه السابق ، كذا في «العناية)) () ساكنة، [٢] قوله : أهل أخبية ؛ بفتح الهمزة ، وكسر الباء الموحدة، بينهما خاء معجمة جمع خباء : بالكسر ، وهو خيمةٌ من صوف ونحوه، وأهل الأخبية : هم الذين يقيمون في المفازة في الأخبية ونحوها كالأعراب والتركمان ونحوهم. [٣]قوله : تصح منهم ؛ لأنَّ عادتهم المقام في المفازة، فكانت في حقهم كالقرى في حق أهل القرى. [٤] قوله : هو الصحيح ؛ قال في «الكفاية»: ذكر في «المبسوط»(۲) : : اختلف المتأخرين في الذين يسكنون الأخبية، فمنهم من يقول : لا يكونون مقيمين أبداً ، والأصح أنهم مقيمون، وعُلّل بوجهين : أحدهما : أنَّ الإقامة أصل والسفر عارض، فحمل حالهم على الأصل أولى.
Book of Prayer: Prayer of the Traveler. He shortens his four-rak'ah obligatory prayer. So he shortens if he intends less than half a month, or intends its duration in two places, or enters a city intending to leave tomorrow or the day after and his stay becomes prolonged, and likewise an army that enters a land of war or besieges a fort therein. He shortens his four-rak'ah obligatory prayer; he shortens if he intends less than half a month, or intends its duration: meaning the duration of stay, which is half a month, in two places, or enters a city intending to leave tomorrow or the day after and his stay becomes prolonged, and likewise an army that enters a land of war or besieges a fort therein. [1] His statement: his four-rak'ah obligatory prayer; as a distinction from the three-rak'ah like Maghrib and the two-rak'ah like Fajr. [2] His statement: in two places; such that he intends in one place ten days, for example, and in another place the remainder, then he is not considered a resident. It is established in the authentic books that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to shorten during the journey of the Farewell Pilgrimage until he returned to Medina, even though he stayed in Makkah, Mina, Arafat, and other places for ten days. (3) His statement: or enters a city... etc.; meaning he enters a city and intends to leave in a day or two, and did not intend a stay of fifteen days, and his stay prolongs there with this intention. It is established from Ibn Umar that he stayed in Azerbaijan for six months and did not complete the prayer, as recorded by Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf, and by al-Bayhaqi in Kitab al-Ma'rifah, and similar from a group of Companions in Sahih Muslim and Sunan al-Bayhaqi and others. [4] His statement: likewise; i.e., an army that enters dar al-harb for warfare, or besieges a fort in dar al-harb, even if they intend a stay of half a month, because they are between fleeing if defeated and staying if victorious, so that place is not their place of stay, so their intention does not take effect, just as it does not take effect in the desert, unlike if a single Muslim enters their land under aman, he completes if he intends to stay for its duration. (295) (1) From Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): that he stayed in Azerbaijan for six months shortening the prayer, and he said: When I am determined on staying, I complete. In Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (2:533), and from Jabir ibn Abdullah: The Messenger of Allah stayed in Tabuk for twenty days shortening the prayer, in Sunan Abi Dawud (1:393) and Musnad Ahmad (3:...), authenticated by our teacher al-Arna'ut, and Sahih Ibn Hibban (6:456), and in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad (1:298): It is narrated from al-Hasan: We were with al-Hasan ibn Samurah in some land of Persia for two years, he did not combine nor increase beyond two rak'ahs. It is narrated that Anas ibn Malik stayed in Syria for two months with Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan praying two rak'ahs. There are other reports mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Rayah. Or the people of baghy in our land outside a misr, even if they intend a stay of its duration. Not the people of tents if they intend it in the most correct view. Or the people of baghy in our land outside a misr, even if they intend a stay of its duration: i.e., the mentioned groups shorten even if they intend a stay of half a month, because they do not become residents by the intention of stay. (Not the people of tents if they intend it in the most correct view): i.e., the people of tents do not shorten if they intend a stay of half a month in their tents, because the intention of stay is valid from them in the desert, because staying is the original condition that is not invalidated by their moving from pasture to pasture. This is the correct view. [1] His statement: or the people of baghy; i.e., the Muslim army besieging the people of baghy—who are those who rebel against the ruler in dar al-Islam—they shorten because they are between staying and fleeing. Although the ruling of this issue is known from what preceded, it is mentioned to refute the misconception that the intention of stay in dar al-harb is not valid because it is severed from dar al-Islam, so it is like a desert, unlike the city of the people of baghy because it is dar al-Islam. So it indicates that the intention of stay is not valid for those besieging them even in dar al-Islam. From this it is known that the restriction to other than a misr is by agreement, for the ruling in besieging a misr is also the same due to the previous reason, as in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: people of tents; with a fatha on the hamza, and kasra on the ba, between them a kha' with a dot, plural of khuba': with kasr, which is a tent of wool and the like. The people of tents are those who stay in the desert in tents and the like, like Bedouins and Turkmens and the like. [3] His statement: is valid from them; because their custom is staying in the desert, so it is in their regard like villages for the people of villages. [4] His statement: this is the correct view; al-Kifayah said: In al-Mabsut it is mentioned: The later scholars differed concerning those who live in tents. Some say they are never residents, and the most correct is that they are residents, and it is reasoned with two aspects: One is that residence is the original condition and travel is incidental, so applying the original condition to their situation is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the conditions for shortening prayer (qasr) in travel, specifically focusing on the effect of intending residence (niyyat al-iqamah). The author begins by reiterating the core ruling: a traveler shortens his four-rak'ah obligatory prayers. The key exception is that if he intends to stay in a place for half a month (15 days) or more, he becomes a 'resident' (muqim) and must pray complete prayers. However, the text clarifies several nuanced scenarios: If he intends less than half a month, he shortens. If he intends a total of half a month but splits it between two locations, he does not become a resident and continues to shorten. If he enters a city with no firm intention to stay long but ends up staying for an extended period, he remains a musafir as long as he does not resolve to stay 15 days. This is supported by the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in Hajjat al-Wada' and by Ibn 'Umar's six-month stay in Azerbaijan. The same ruling applies to soldiers in dar al-harb or besieging a fort: even if they intend to stay half a month, they shorten because their situation is unstable. This is contrasted with a civilian Muslim entering dar al-harb under aman—if he intends to stay, he must complete his prayers. The text then addresses two additional groups: (1) the army besieging rebels (ahl al-baghy) in dar al-Islam: they too shorten, even if they intend 15 days, because they are also in a state of uncertainty. This is mentioned to avoid the misunderstanding that only dar al-harb prevents intention of residence. (2) Tent-dwellers (ahl al-akhbiyah) like Bedouins: the correct view is that if they intend to stay in their tents for half a month, they become residents and must complete their prayers. This is because residence is their norm; travel is exceptional for them. The commentary then adds that among later Hanafi scholars, there was an ikhtilaf: some held that such people are never residents, but the relied-upon position is that they are residents if they intend to stay.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah obligatory prayers to two rak'ahs while traveling.
إقامة (iqamah): Intention to stay in a place for half a month (15 days) or more, which ends the traveler's status and makes complete prayer obligatory.
أهل أخبية (ahl akhbiyah): People who live in tents or portable dwellings in the desert, like Bedouins and Turkmens.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following travelers must pray complete (tamam) prayers?
A) A soldier besieging a fort in dar al-harb who intends to stay for 20 days.
B) A traveler who intends to stay in one city for 14 days.
C) A Bedouin (ahl akhbiyah) who intends to stay in his tent for half a month.
D) A merchant who enters a city intending to leave the next day but ends up staying for a month without changing his intention.

Key Takeaway
A traveler shortens prayers unless he intends to stay half a month (15 days) or more in one place, with exceptions for soldiers and those in uncertain situations, while tent-dwellers are considered residents when they intend the duration.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
The Tent-Dweller (Khaba'i): Does He Shorten Prayer?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tent-dweller qasr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relied-upon Hanafi position on whether a tent-dweller (khaba'i) shortens prayer and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue.

Core Idea
The tent-dweller does not shorten prayer because his movement is not a legal journey.

In this lesson, we examine the special case of the tent-dweller (khaba'i) who moves with his livestock from pasture to pasture. While some later Hanafi scholars argued he should shorten, the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that he prays complete. We will trace the reasoning behind this position and explore the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Define the legal traveler
State the Hanafi definition: a traveler is one who leaves his hometown intending a journey of three days and nights at moderate speed. This triggers the concession to shorten four-rak'ah prayers.
The journey must be permissible and not for sin.
2
Identify the tent-dweller's situation
The khaba'i lives in a tent (khaimah) and moves from one grazing ground to another. He does not intend a fixed-distance journey; he relocates within his customary range.
This is the core distinction: his movement lacks the intention of a legal safar.
3
State the two positions
Some later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) said the khaba'i shortens because he is not in a permanent residence. The majority and the relied-upon view say he does not shorten because his movement is not a legal journey.
The tarjih is clearly for the second view, as per al-Kirmani, al-Mudmarat, and al-Khazanah.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The khaba'i's movement is a change of grazing grounds, not a journey for which qasr was legislated. Qasr is for those who travel a significant distance away from their home, not for those who simply relocate within their habitual range.
This is a dalil aqli based on the purpose of the concession.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
If a tent-dweller undertakes a genuine long-distance journey (e.g., to a city), he then shortens. But his routine moves between pastures do not count as travel.
Practical consequence: he always prays complete unless he undertakes a trip of three days' distance.

[1]قوله : والحال ؛ أشار به إلى أنَّ الواو في قوله : «وهو خبائي» حالية. (٢) قوله : فإنه جعل نية الإقامة...الخ ؛ يعني قال قبله : «فيقصر الرباعي إلى أن يدخل...» الخ، فجعل غاية القصرِ دخول بيوت بلدة ، ونيَّة الإقامة مدتها ببلدة واحدة أو قرية واحدة، أو بصحراء دارنا حالَ كونه خبائياً ، ومن المعلوم أنَّ حكم الغاية يخالف حكم المغيا، فيفيد كلامه أن لا يقصر الداخل بلده والناوي إقامة ببلد، أو قرية أو بصحراء دارنا ، والأخير مختص بالخبائي. [٣] قوله : نفي لذلك النفي ؛ حاصلُهُ : أَنَّهُ ثبت من سابق كلامه نفي القصر لِمَن ينوي الإقامة، وهو خبائي بصحراء دار الإسلام، وعطف عليه قوله : «لا بدار الحرب» بكلمة النفي يكون ذلك نفياً لذلك النفي، ونفي النفي إثبات، فيفيدُ أنَّ المحاصر بدار الحرب ومحاصر أهل البغي يقصر.
[٤] قوله : لما فهم.... الخ ؛ بيان لوجه اتصال قوله : كمن طال مكثه، بما سبق. [0]قوله : في بلدة أو قرية ؛ اكتفى بذكرهما ؛ لأنَّ المكث فيهما هو الذي يختلج فيه أنه لا يقصر ، وأما القصر في مكث الصحراء فالأمر ظاهر. [٦] قوله : فلو أتم ؛ اختلف العلماء في أنَّ قصر السفرِ في الصَّلاةِ رخصة أم عزيمة
Book of Prayer: Prayer of the Traveler. It is said: their intention of residence is not valid, for residence is only valid in cities or villages. And the wording of al-Mukhtasar: 'And in the desert of our land, and he is a tent-dweller, not in Dar al-Harb, or a besieger in Dar al-Baghy, like one whose stay is prolonged without intention.' And the second: that travel only occurs when moving to a place for the duration of travel, and they never intend the duration of travel, but rather move from water to water and from pasture to pasture. [1] His saying: 'And the wording of al-Mukhtasar...' etc.; he mentioned it because it is more concise and because of the obscurity in it, so he wanted to clarify it. The complete phrasing: 'Thus the traveler is he who leaves the houses of his town intending a distance of three days and nights with moderate travel, which is what camels, foot-travelers, and ships when the wind is moderate, and what suits horses, then he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer; i.e., until he enters the houses of his hometown, or intends residence of half a month in a town or one village, or in the desert of our land while he is a tent-dweller... etc.' [2] His saying: 'And he is a tent-dweller'; he said in Jami' al-Rumuz: 'That is, while the one being addressed is among those who dwell in the desert like Bedouins, Turks, Kurds, Turkomans, and shepherds who roam over pastures, then he does not shorten and prays complete, as some later scholars said, because he moves from pasture to pasture. And it is said: he shortens here as well, because it is not a place of residence. The first is more correct, as in al-Kirmani, and upon it is the fatwa, as in al-Mudmarat and al-Khazanah. And it contains an indication that the one intending in the desert or other than a tent-dweller shortens.' [3] His saying: 'Not in Dar al-Harb'; al-Burjandi said in his commentary: 'i.e., he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer, not until he intends residence in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy as a besieger against the people of war or the people of rebellion. The negation of these being a limit for shortening indicates that shortening is not permissible for the besieger in them. The apparent meaning is that by besieger in Dar al-Harb is meant one who is engaged in fighting, whether he is actually besieging or not.' He said in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'The principle in this is that the intention of residence, if it is in a place where he can reside by his own choice, makes him a resident; otherwise no. So if Muslims besiege a city in Dar al-Harb, or they descend in some of their houses and fight them, and they intend residence there, it is not valid.' [4] His saying: 'Like one whose stay is prolonged'; it is attached to what is understood from the previous speech; i.e., the besieger in Dar al-Harb shortens a shortening like the shortening of one whose stay in a place of residence is prolonged without intention of residence, but rather he intends to leave within less than fifteen days.' (1) i.e., al-Niqayah (p. 36). Then if a traveler prays complete [four rak'ahs] and sits in the first [after two rak'ahs], his fard is fulfilled and he is sinful. 'Umda al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya: 'i.e., he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer until he intends residence in the desert of our land while he is a tent-dweller: i.e., one of the people of the tent, which is the tent, then he does not shorten. For the intention of residence in the desert of our land is valid for him; but for other than the people of the tent, if they intend residence in the desert of our land, it is not valid. So it is understood from this that whoever besieges the people of rebellion in our land, his intention of residence is not valid if he is in the desert. And his saying: 'Not in Dar al-Harb' is a conjunctive to his saying 'in the desert of our land', for he made the intention of residence in the desert of our land a limit for shortening, and the ruling of the limit is contrary to the ruling of the limited, so its ruling is not shortening. Then his saying: 'Not in Dar al-Harb or a besieger in Dar al-Baghy' is a negation of that negation, so its ruling is shortening: i.e., he shortens if he intends residence of half a month in Dar al-Harb as a besieger there. And his saying: 'Like one whose stay is prolonged without intention'; from what is understood from his saying 'Not in Dar al-Harb', the ruling of shortening, he said: 'Like one whose stay is prolonged', i.e., he shortens one whose stay in a town or village is prolonged without intention of staying. [4] (Then if a traveler prays complete [four] and sits in the first, his fard is fulfilled and he is sinful) due to delaying the salutation. [1] His saying: 'And while'; he pointed out that the waw in his saying 'and he is a tent-dweller' is a circumstantial waw. [2] His saying: 'For he made the intention of residence... etc.'; meaning he said before: 'So he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer until he enters...' etc., so he made the limit of shortening the entering of the houses of the town, and the intention of residence for its duration in one town or one village, or in the desert of our land while being a tent-dweller. And it is known that the ruling of the limit contradicts the ruling of the limited, so his speech indicates that the one who enters his hometown and the one who intends residence in a town or village or in the desert of our land does not shorten, and the last is specific to the tent-dweller. [3] His saying: 'A negation of that negation'; its conclusion: that it is established from his previous speech the negation of shortening for one who intends residence while being a tent-dweller in the desert of Dar al-Islam, and he appended to it his saying 'not in Dar al-Harb' with the word of negation, so that is a negation of that negation, and the negation of negation is affirmation, so it indicates that the besieger in Dar al-Harb and the besieger of the people of rebellion shorten. [4] His saying: 'From what is understood... etc.'; explanation of the connection of his saying 'like one whose stay is prolonged' to what preceded. [0] His saying: 'in a town or village'; he sufficed with mentioning them because the stay in them is what causes doubt that he does not shorten, while shortening in the stay in the desert is apparent. [6] His saying: 'Then if he prays complete'; the scholars differed over whether the shortening of prayer in travel is a concession or an obligation.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, discussing the rulings for shortening prayer (qasr) for travelers. The passage covers three main areas: (1) the definition of a traveler who shortens, (2) the special case of the tent-dweller (khaba'i) who moves with his livestock, and (3) the ruling for a traveler who prays a complete four-rak'ah prayer and sits for tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs.

The commentary begins by clarifying the matn's wording. A traveler is defined as someone who leaves his hometown intending a journey of three days and nights at moderate speed. Such a traveler shortens the four-rak'ah prayers until one of three things happens: he enters his hometown, he intends to stay for fifteen days (half a month) in a town or village, or he intends residence in the desert of his own land while being a tent-dweller. The tent-dweller here refers to Bedouins, Turks, Kurds, Turkomans, and shepherds who wander from pasture to pasture. There is a dispute: some later scholars say the tent-dweller does not shorten because he simply moves from one grazing area to another, not truly traveling a fixed distance. Others say he still shortens because his dwelling place is not a permanent residence. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the tent-dweller does not shorten; he prays complete prayers. This is the position of al-Kirmani and is upheld in al-Mudmarat and al-Khazanah.

Next, the commentary addresses the case of a besieger in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy. If a Muslim is besieging enemy territory or rebels, his intention of residence is not valid because he cannot freely choose to reside there. Therefore, he continues to shorten his prayers even if he stays a long time. The ruling is clarified: the besieger shortens like one who stays without the intention of staying for fifteen days; he does not become a resident.

Finally, the passage addresses a separate ruling: if a traveler prays four rak'ahs (i.e., prays the complete prayer instead of shortening) and sits for tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs, his fard (obligatory two rak'ahs) is fulfilled, but he is sinful because he delayed the salutation unnecessarily. This indicates that while the prayer is valid, it is makruh to lengthen it without cause. The commentary then notes that scholars differ on whether qasr is a concession (rukhsah) or an obligation (azimah), but this passage does not resolve that debate.

Key Terms
خبائي (khabā'ī): A tent-dweller, one who lives in a tent (khaimah) and moves with his livestock for grazing.
قصر (qaṣr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayers to two rak'ahs while traveling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A tent-dweller (khaba'i) sets out from his usual grazing area intending to move to another pasture 100 km away. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, does he shorten his prayers?
A) Yes, because he is traveling.
B) No, because his movement is not considered a legal journey.
C) Yes, but only if he intends to stay less than 15 days.
D) No, because he is in Dar al-Harb.

Key Takeaway
The khaba'i does not shorten prayer because his routine grazing moves are not a legal journey.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
7 min
The Besieger and Prayer Completion While Traveling
Rulings on qasr for besieger and on completing prayer while traveling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a besieger in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy shortens prayer, and explain the ruling if a traveler prays four rak'ahs with tashahhud after two.

Core Idea
A besieger shortens even with a long stay; a traveler who prays four rak'ahs and sits after two has fulfilled his fard but committed a sin.

This lesson covers two distinct rulings from the passage: first, the special case of the besieger (muhasir) who is in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy – he continues to shorten his prayers because his intention of residence is invalid. Second, a procedural ruling: if a traveler prays four rak'ahs (complete) and sits for tashahhud after the first two, his fard rak'ahs are valid but he incurs sin for delaying the salutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the besieger's situation
The besieger is in enemy or rebel territory, unable to settle freely. In Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy, he cannot validly intend residence.
This is key: free choice is a condition for valid intention of residence.
2
Understand the principle of intention of residence
For qasr to end, one must intend residence (iqama) for 15 days in a place where that intention is valid. In Dar al-Harb, intention is invalid because the resident cannot freely choose to stay.
Thus, the besieger never becomes a resident.
3
Apply to the besieger
The besieger shortens his prayers even if he stays for months, as long as he has not intended to stay 15 days in a valid residence.
This matches the principle.
4
Examine the case of completing prayer
If a traveler prays four rak'ahs (instead of two) and sits for tashahhud after the second rak'ah, his obligatory two rak'ahs are fulfilled because he completed the required sitting. However, he is sinful for unnecessarily lengthening the prayer.
The prayer is valid but makruh.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on qasr being rukhsa or azima
The passage ends by noting that scholars differ on whether qasr is a concession (rukhsah) or an obligation (azimah), but does not resolve it. This does not affect the mufta bihi rulings.
For fatwa, qasr is a confirmed sunnah.

[4] (Then if a traveler prays complete [four] and sits in the first, his fard is fulfilled and he is sinful) due to delaying the salutation. [1] His saying: 'And while'; he pointed out that the waw in his saying 'and he is a tent-dweller' is a circumstantial waw. [2] His saying: 'For he made the intention of residence... etc.'; meaning he said before: 'So he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer until he enters...' etc., so he made the limit of shortening the entering of the houses of the town, and the intention of residence for its duration in one town or one village, or in the desert of our land while being a tent-dweller. And it is known that the ruling of the limit contradicts the ruling of the limited, so his speech indicates that the one who enters his hometown and the one who intends residence in a town or village or in the desert of our land does not shorten, and the last is specific to the tent-dweller. [3] His saying: 'A negation of that negation'; its conclusion: that it is established from his previous speech the negation of shortening for one who intends residence while being a tent-dweller in the desert of Dar al-Islam, and he appended to it his saying 'not in Dar al-Harb' with the word of negation, so that is a negation of that negation, and the negation of negation is affirmation, so it indicates that the besieger in Dar al-Harb and the besieger of the people of rebellion shorten. [4] His saying: 'From what is understood... etc.'; explanation of the connection of his saying 'like one whose stay is prolonged' to what preceded. [0] His saying: 'in a town or village'; he sufficed with mentioning them because the stay in them is what causes doubt that he does not shorten, while shortening in the stay in the desert is apparent. [6] His saying: 'Then if he prays complete'; the scholars differed over whether the shortening of prayer in travel is a concession or an obligation.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, discussing the rulings for shortening prayer (qasr) for travelers. The passage covers three main areas: (1) the definition of a traveler who shortens, (2) the special case of the tent-dweller (khaba'i) who moves with his livestock, and (3) the ruling for a traveler who prays a complete four-rak'ah prayer and sits for tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs.

The commentary begins by clarifying the matn's wording. A traveler is defined as someone who leaves his hometown intending a journey of three days and nights at moderate speed. Such a traveler shortens the four-rak'ah prayers until one of three things happens: he enters his hometown, he intends to stay for fifteen days (half a month) in a town or village, or he intends residence in the desert of his own land while being a tent-dweller. The tent-dweller here refers to Bedouins, Turks, Kurds, Turkomans, and shepherds who wander from pasture to pasture. There is a dispute: some later scholars say the tent-dweller does not shorten because he simply moves from one grazing area to another, not truly traveling a fixed distance. Others say he still shortens because his dwelling place is not a permanent residence. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the tent-dweller does not shorten; he prays complete prayers. This is the position of al-Kirmani and is upheld in al-Mudmarat and al-Khazanah.

Next, the commentary addresses the case of a besieger in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Baghy. If a Muslim is besieging enemy territory or rebels, his intention of residence is not valid because he cannot freely choose to reside there. Therefore, he continues to shorten his prayers even if he stays a long time. The ruling is clarified: the besieger shortens like one who stays without the intention of staying for fifteen days; he does not become a resident.

Finally, the passage addresses a separate ruling: if a traveler prays four rak'ahs (i.e., prays the complete prayer instead of shortening) and sits for tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs, his fard (obligatory two rak'ahs) is fulfilled, but he is sinful because he delayed the salutation unnecessarily. This indicates that while the prayer is valid, it is makruh to lengthen it without cause. The commentary then notes that scholars differ on whether qasr is a concession (rukhsah) or an obligation (azimah), but this passage does not resolve that debate.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): A territory under non-Muslim control where the laws of Islam are not enforced.
دار البغي (dār al-baghy): Territory of rebels against a just Muslim ruler.
إقامة (iqāmah): Intention to reside in a place for fifteen days or more, which ends the traveler's concession to shorten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim soldier is stationed in a non-Muslim country (Dar al-Harb) for six months on a military mission. He does not intend to settle permanently. According to Hanafi fiqh, does he shorten his prayers?
A) No, because he has been there more than 15 days.
B) Yes, because his intention of residence is invalid in Dar al-Harb.
C) No, because he is not traveling.
D) Yes, but only for the first 15 days.

Key Takeaway
A besieger in Dar al-Harb/Dar al-Baghy shortens prayer because he cannot validly intend residence; a traveler who prays four rak'ahs and sits after two has fulfilled his fard but is sinful.

Qasr Prayer: Concession or Determination? The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on qasr with proofs and practical consequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qasr, explain why it is an azimah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of Aisha and the analogy with sa'y.

Core Idea
Qasr is the obligatory fard for the traveler; completing four rak'ahs is a sin.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah tackles one of the most famous inter-madhhab disputes: Is shortening prayer (qasr) on a journey a concession (rukhsah) that allows completion, or a determination (azimah) that must be followed? The Hanafi school holds the latter, and the text provides both the Shafi'i proof from the Qur'an and the Hanafi rebuttal using hadith and qiyas. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to weigh dalil and recognize the thamarah al-khilaf in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says about qasr: is it a concession or a determination? What is the rukhsah/azimah distinction?
The Hanafis say qasr is azimah; the traveler's fard is two rak'ahs.
2
Identify the opponent and their dalil
The Shafi'i school holds qasr is a rukhsah. Their primary proof is Qur'an 4:101 using 'la junah' (no sin). Note how they interpret this as permissibility.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafis bring hadith from Aisha, Ibn Abbas, and Umar stating that prayer was originally two rak'ahs in travel. Understand how these hadith show obligation.
These hadith are from Bukhari, Muslim, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
The Hanafis argue that 'la junah' does not negate obligation, using the analogy of sa'y between Safa and Marwa, which is obligatory by consensus despite similar wording.
The key is that 'la junah' indicates removal of sin, not that the act is optional.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? According to the Hanafis, if a traveler stands for the third rak'ah without sitting, his prayer becomes invalid. According to the Shafi'is, completion is valid and better.
This shows the importance of the dispute in actual prayer.

فلو أتم مسافر، وقعد في الأولى، تم فرضه وأساء
عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
أي يقصر الرباعي إلى أن ينوي الإقامة بصحراء دارنا ، والحال أنه خبائي : أي من أهل الخباء، وهو الخَيْمة، فإنَّه لا يقصر ، فإنَّ نية الإقامة في صحراء دارنا صحيحة، أما غير أهل الخباء لو نَوَى الإقامة في صحراء دارنا لا يصح، فَعُلِمَ منه أنَّ مَن حاصر أهل البَغْي في دارنا لا يصح منه نية الإقامة إذا كان في الصحراء. وقوله : لا بدار الحرب ؛ عطف على قوله بصحراء دارنا فإنَّه جعل نيَّةَ الإقامة في صحراء دَارِنا غايةً للقصر، وحكمُ الغاية مخالف لحكم المغَيَّا، فيكون حكمه عدم القصر. ثُمَّ قوله : لا بدار الحرب أو البغي مُحاصِراً، نفي لذلك النفي، فيكون حكمُهُ القصر : أي يقصر إن نَوَى إقامة نِصْفِ شَهْر بدار الحرب محاصراً لذلك. وقوله : كمن طال مكتُهُ بلا نيَّة ؛ لما فهم من قوله : لا بدار الحرب ؛ حُكْمُ القصر قال : كمَن طال مكثه ، أي يقصرُ مَن طَالَ مُكْتُهُ في بلدة ، أو قرية بلا نيَّة المكث
[٤]
فلو أتم مسافر ، وقعد في الأولى، تم فرضه وأساء لتأخير السلام
فائدة الخلاف : تظهرُ في افتراض القعدة على رأس الركعتين من الرباعية، حتى لو قام إلى الثالثة من غير قصد فسدت صلاته عندنا.

اختلف العلماء في أنَّ قصر السفرِ في الصَّلاةِ رخصة أم عزيمة : فذهب الشافعي له ، وغيره إلى أنَّ فرض المسافرِ أربع ركعات، والقصر رخصة. وذهب أصحابنا إلى أنَّ الفرضَ في حقه ركعتان، والقصر عزيمة. فعنده الإتمام والقصر جائزان، والأفضل هو الإتمام. وعندنا لا بد أن يقصر، فلو صلى أربع ركعات أساء. وحجة من قال بكون القصر رخصة ، قوله : وَإِذَا ضَرَبْتُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلوة )، فإنّه شرع القصر بلفظ لا جناح ، فدل على أنه مباح لا واجب. ولنا : حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت: «فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين فأُقرت في السفر، وزيدت في الحضر»، أخرجه الشيخان وغيرهما. وعند مسلم وغيره عن ابن عباس : «فرض اللهُ الصَّلاةَ على لسان نبيكُم في الحضر أربع ركعات، وفي السَّفْرِ ركعتين». وعند النسائي وابن ماجة عن عمر الله : «صلاةُ السَّفر ركعتان، وصلاة الضحى وصلاة الفطر ركعتان تمام غير قصر على لسان محمد». والجواب عن الآية : أنه لا يستلزم عدم الإساءة بدليل أنه قال في حق السعي بين الصفا والمروة : إنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَارِ اللَّهِ فَمَنْ حَجَّ الْبَيْتَ أَوِ اعْتَمَرَ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِ أَن يَطَّوفَ بِهِمَا ، مع أنَّ الطُّواف بهما واجب وركن اتفاقاً.
If a traveler completes [the prayer] and sits in the first [two rak'ahs], his obligatory prayer is fulfilled and he has done wrong. Explanation: i.e., he shortens the four-rak'ah prayer until he intends to stay in the desert of our land while being a tent-dweller — meaning one of the people of the tent, which is the tent — for then he does not shorten. Indeed, the intention to stay in the desert of our land is valid. As for one who is not a tent-dweller, if he intends to stay in the desert of our land, it is not valid. So it is understood from this that one who besieges the people of rebellion in our land cannot validly intend to stay if in the desert. And his statement: 'not in the abode of war' is a conjunction with his statement 'in the desert of our land', for he made the intention to stay in the desert of our land a limit for shortening, and the ruling of the limit is contrary to the ruling of the limited, so its ruling is not shortening. Then his statement: 'not in the abode of war or rebellion while besieging' is a negation of that negation, so its ruling is shortening: i.e., he may shorten if he intends to stay half a month in the abode of war while besieging it. And his statement: 'like one whose stay is prolonged without intention' — because from his statement 'not in the abode of war' the ruling of shortening was understood, he said: 'like one whose stay is prolonged,' i.e., one whose stay in a town or village is prolonged without intending to stay [shortens]. [4] So if a traveler completes [the prayer] and sits in the first [two rak'ahs], his obligatory prayer is fulfilled and he has done wrong due to delaying the salutation. The benefit of the dispute appears in the obligation of the sitting at the end of two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer, so that if he stands up for the third without intention, his prayer becomes invalid according to us. The scholars differed as to whether shortening prayer on a journey is a concession or a determination: Imam al-Shafi'i and others went that the traveler's obligatory prayer is four rak'ahs, and shortening is a concession. And our companions went that the obligatory prayer in his case is two rak'ahs, and shortening is a determination. According to him, completion and shortening are both permissible, and the better is completion. According to us, he must shorten; if he prays four rak'ahs, he does wrong. The proof of those who said shortening is a concession is His statement: 'And when you travel in the land, there is no sin upon you that you shorten the prayer,' for He legislated shortening with the phrase 'no sin,' indicating that it is permissible, not obligatory. And our proof: the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs, two rak'ahs; it was confirmed in travel and increased in residence,' reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others. And according to Muslim and others from Ibn Abbas: 'Allah prescribed prayer on the tongue of your Prophet in residence as four rak'ahs, and in travel as two rak'ahs.' And according to al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The prayer of travel is two rak'ahs, the prayer of Duha is two rak'ahs, and the prayer of Fitr is two rak'ahs — complete, not shortened — on the tongue of Muhammad.' The response to the verse: it does not necessitate the absence of wrongdoing by the evidence that He said concerning the sa'i between Safa and Marwa: 'Indeed, Safa and Marwa are among the symbols of Allah. So whoever performs Hajj or Umrah, there is no sin upon him to go around them,' while circumambulating them is obligatory and a pillar by consensus.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah is a commentary on al-Wiqayah dealing with the prayer of a traveler. It begins by explaining the conditions under which a traveler may shorten the four-rak'ah prayers. The key point is that intention of residence in one's own desert as a tent-dweller (khabā'ī) is valid, but otherwise not. The commentary then addresses the ruling that if a traveler completes the prayer (i.e., prays four rak'ahs) but sits after two rak'ahs for the tashahhud, his prayer is valid but he is sinful for delaying the salām. This then leads into the major inter-madhhab dispute: Is shortening (qasr) on a journey a concession (rukhsah) or a determination (azīmah)? The Shāfi'ī school holds that the traveler's fard is four rak'ahs and qasr is a concession, so both are permissible though completion is better. The Hanafī school holds that the fard is two rak'ahs, so qasr is a determination; the traveler must shorten, and to pray four rak'ahs is sinful. The Shāfi'īs cite the Qur'ānic verse using 'lā junāh' (no sin) to argue permissibility. The Hanafīs respond with multiple hadiths from ʿĀ'ishah, Ibn ʿAbbās, and ʿUmar (raḍī Allāhu ʿanhum) stating that prayer was originally two rak'ahs in travel. They argue that 'lā junāh' does not negate obligation, as seen in the similar phrasing for saʿī between Safa and Marwah, which is obligatory by consensus. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is the obligation of sitting after two rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer for a traveler: if he stands for the third without intending, his prayer becomes invalid according to the Hanafīs, whereas according to the Shāfi'īs it would be valid.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah obligatory prayers to two rak'ahs while traveling.
رخصة (rukhsah): A legal ruling that is more lenient than the original due to a valid excuse.
عزيمة (azimah): The original, strict ruling of law that is always applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The prayer is valid and he is rewarded for completing.
B) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
C) The prayer is valid but he has committed a sin.
D) The prayer is valid and he has done nothing wrong.

Key Takeaway
For the Hanafi school, qasr is an obligatory determination (azimah) for the traveler; completing to four rak'ahs is sinful, and the practical difference lies in the invalidity of standing for the third rak'ah without sitting.

Qasr: Concession or Obligation? (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
Debate on whether qasr is a concession or obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that qasr is an obligation (azimah) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
The phrase 'no blame' does not negate obligation; just as sa'i is obligatory despite 'no blame', qasr is wajib.

This lesson presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of shortening prayer (qasr) during travel. The core question is whether qasr is a strict obligation (azimah) or a mere concession (rukhsah). The difference has practical consequences for the traveler's prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Shafi'i and Hanafi views on the obligation of the traveler. The Shafi'i say the obligation is four rak'ahs and qasr is a concession; the Hanafis say the obligation is two rak'ahs and qasr is an obligation.
The core disagreement is whether the traveler's 'default' is four or two.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Examine the Shafi'i reliance on Quran 4:101, which uses the phrase 'there is no blame upon you' (la junah). Understand why they argue this indicates permissibility.
The Shafi'i interpret 'la junah' as a sign of permissibility, not obligation.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Review the Hanafi counter: the same phrase appears in 2:158 regarding sa'i between Safa and Marwah, yet sa'i is obligatory by consensus. Also, consider the hadiths from Aisha, Ibn Abbas, and Umar explicitly stating the travel prayer as two rak'ahs.
The Hanafis use analogy (qiyas) to show 'la junah' does not negate obligation.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: according to Hanafis, the sitting after two rak'ahs is obligatory; omitting it invalidates the prayer. According to Shafi'is, both completing and shortening are valid.
This practical difference affects the validity of the traveler's prayer.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon) in the school: the hadith evidence is explicit and the Quranic phrase is interpreted in light of other evidence.
The Hanafi position is supported by multiple authentic hadiths.

وشبهة عدم قبول صدقة الله تعالى فذهب الشافعي له ، وغيره إلى أنَّ فرض المسافرِ أربع ركعات، والقصر رخصة. وذهب أصحابنا إلى أنَّ الفرضَ في حقه ركعتان، والقصر عزيمة. فعنده الإتمام والقصر جائزان، والأفضل هو الإتمام. وعندنا لا بد أن يقصر، فلو صلى أربع ركعات أساء. فائدة الخلاف : تظهرُ في افتراض القعدة على رأس الركعتين من الرباعية، حتى لو قام إلى الثالثة من غير قصد فسدت صلاته عندنا. وحجة من قال بكون القصر رخصة ، قوله : وَإِذَا ضَرَبْتُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلوة ) (۱) ، فإنّه شرع القصر بلفظ لا جناح ، فدل على أنه مباح لا واجب. ولنا : حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت: «فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين فأُقرت في السفر، وزيدت في الحضر» (۳)، أخرجه الشيخان وغيرهما. وعند مسلم وغيره عن ابن عباس : «فرض اللهُ الصَّلاةَ على لسان نبيكُم في الحضر أربع ركعات، وفي السَّفْرِ (ركعتين) (۳) وعند النسائي وابن ماجة عن عمر الله : «صلاةُ السَّفر ركعتان، وصلاة الضحى وصلاة الفطر ركعتان تمام غير قصر على لسان محمد (4). والجواب عن الآية : أنه لا يستلزم عدم الإساءة بدليل أنه قال في حق السعي بين الصفا والمروة : إنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَارِ اللَّهِ فَمَنْ حَجَّ الْبَيْتَ أَوِ اعْتَمَرَ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِ أَن يَطَّوفَ بِهِمَا ) ، مع أنَّ الطُّواف بهما واجب وركن اتفاقاً.
And the doubt about not accepting the charity of Allah Almighty. So al-Shafi'i and others went that the obligation of the traveler is four rak'ahs, and shortening is a concession. And our companions (Hanafis) went that the obligation in his case is two rak'ahs, and shortening is a resolve (azimah). According to him, completing and shortening are both permissible, but completing is better. According to us, he must shorten; if he prays four rak'ahs, he does wrong. The benefit of the difference appears in the obligation of the sitting at the end of two rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer, such that if he stands for the third without intention, his prayer becomes invalid according to us. And the proof of those who said shortening is a concession is His statement: 'And when you travel in the land, there is no blame upon you for shortening the prayer' (4:101), for He legislated shortening using the phrase 'no blame', indicating it is permissible, not obligatory. And our proof: the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs two rak'ahs, then it was confirmed in travel and increased in residence' (narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others). And according to Muslim and others from Ibn Abbas: 'Allah prescribed prayer on the tongue of your Prophet in residence as four rak'ahs, and in travel as two rak'ahs.' And according to al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The prayer of travel is two rak'ahs, and the prayer of Duha and the prayer of Fitr are two rak'ahs, complete, not shortened, on the tongue of Muhammad.' And the response to the verse: It does not necessitate the absence of blame; by evidence that He said regarding the sa'i between Safa and Marwah: 'Indeed, as-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah. So whoever performs Hajj or 'Umrah, there is no blame upon him to perform tawaf between them' (2:158), while tawaf between them is obligatory and a pillar by consensus.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the nature of shortening prayer (qasr) during travel. The Shafi'i school holds that the obligation for a traveler is four rak'ahs, and shortening is a concession (rukhsah) that is permissible but not obligatory. The Hanafi school holds that the obligation for a traveler is two rak'ahs, and shortening is a strict obligation (azimah). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that, according to the Hanafis, if a traveler prays four rak'ahs, he commits a sin, and the sitting after the first two rak'ahs becomes obligatory; if he omits it, his prayer is invalid. The Shafi'is argue from the Quranic verse 4:101, which uses the phrase 'there is no blame' (la junah), indicating permissibility, not obligation. The Hanafis respond with several authentic hadiths: the hadith of Aisha states that prayer was originally prescribed as two rak'ahs and was maintained in travel; the hadith of Ibn Abbas explicitly states that Allah prescribed four in residence and two in travel; the hadith of Umar explicitly calls the travel prayer 'complete' (tamam). They also refute the Shafi'i interpretation of the verse by noting that the same expression 'la junah' is used in the Quran for the sa'i between Safa and Marwah, yet the sa'i is obligatory by consensus. Thus, the use of 'la junah' does not indicate mere permissibility.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the obligatory prayer from four rak'ahs to two rak'ahs during travel.
عزيمة (azimah): A strict obligation; the original ruling applied without concession.
رخصة (rukhsah): A concession that permits an exception from the original ruling under certain circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on shortening prayer (qasr) for a traveler?
A) It is a concession (rukhsah) and optional.
B) It is an obligation (azimah) and must be performed.
C) It is forbidden to shorten; one must complete four rak'ahs.
D) It is recommended but not required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds qasr as an obligation, supported by explicit hadiths and the principle that 'no blame' does not indicate permissibility.

Extra Rak'ahs in Traveler's Prayer are Nafl, Sitting is Fard
Ruling on traveler's prayer: extra rak'ahs are nafl, missing sitting invalidates fard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a traveler who prays more than two rak'ahs and explain why omitting the sitting after the second rak'ah invalidates his fard.

Core Idea
For a traveler, the first two rak'ahs are fard; extra rak'ahs are nafl, and missing the sitting after the second invalidates the fard.

This passage from the matn clarifies an important ruling for travelers. When a traveler prays four rak'ahs, only the first two constitute his obligatory prayer (fard); the additional two are supererogatory (nafl). Crucially, he must perform the sitting (qa'dah) after the second rak'ah. If he omits this sitting, his fard becomes invalid, even if he completes the additional rak'ahs. Understanding this ruling ensures the traveler's prayer is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The locus of this ruling is the traveler (musafir) who prays a four-rak'ah prayer, such as Zuhr, Asr, or Isha.
The traveler's fard is reduced to two rak'ahs by qasr.
2
Classify the rak'ahs
Recognize that the first two rak'ahs are the fard (obligatory), and any additional rak'ahs are nafl (supererogatory).
This classification is based on the nature of qasr: the traveler's fard is only two rak'ahs.
3
Recognize the consequence of omitting the sitting
If the traveler does not sit after the second rak'ah, his fard is invalid because the sitting (qa'dah) is fard for him.
The omission of a fard element invalidates the act entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زادَ نفل ، وإن لم يقعد بطل فرضُه (وما زاد ،نفل ، وإن لم يقعد بطل فرضه)؛ لترك القعدة، وهي فرض عليه.
And whatever is added (beyond two) is nafl, and if he does not sit, his fard is invalid. (This is) because of leaving the sitting, and it is fard upon him.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn explaining the consequences of a traveler praying more than two rak'ahs. According to the Hanafi school, when a traveler prays four rak'ahs, the first two rak'ahs constitute his fard, and the additional two rak'ahs are considered nafl (supererogatory). However, since the traveler's fard is only two rak'ahs, he must perform the sitting (qa'dah) after the second rak'ah. If he does not sit after two rak'ahs and instead stands for a third, his fard is invalidated because omission of the obligatory sitting nullifies the fard. The commentator adds that the sitting is fard upon him (the traveler) because the two-rak'ah fard requires a sitting at its completion. This ruling is specific to the traveler; a resident praying four rak'ahs would have his fard in the four rak'ahs and the sitting after two is not fard but wajib.

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer; extra rak'ahs beyond the obligatory ones in a traveler's prayer.
قعدة (qa'dah): The sitting after the second rak'ah in a prayer of two or more rak'ahs.
فرض (fard): An obligation that is essential for the validity of the prayer; its omission invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler prays four rak'ahs without sitting after the second rak'ah, what happens to his prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) His entire prayer becomes invalid.
B) His fard is invalid, but the nafl remains.
C) The prayer is valid but he must repeat it.
D) The prayer is valid and he has not sinned.

Key Takeaway
For a traveler, the sitting after two rak'ahs is fard; omitting it invalidates the obligatory prayer.

Traveler Following a Resident Imam: The Ruling on Completing Four Rak'ahs
Ruling on traveler following a resident imam within and after the time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler following a resident imam and identify the conditions under which he must complete four rak'ahs versus when he may not follow.

Core Idea
When a traveler joins a resident imam within time, he must complete four rak'ahs; after time, he cannot follow.

Many travelers assume they may always shorten their prayers, even when following a resident imam. The Hanafi school clarifies that by choosing to follow a resident, the traveler's obligation becomes full (four rak'ahs). This lesson unpacks the ruling, the exceptions, and the underlying reasoning based on the hadith 'The imam is only appointed to be followed.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Setting
Recognize the scenario: a traveler (musafir) follows a resident (muqim) imam in a 4-rak'ah prayer (like Zuhr, Asr, or Isha). Note the time: within the prescribed time or after.
2
State the Ruling for Within Time
The traveling follower must complete four rak'ahs (itmam). It becomes wajib because by opting to follow the resident, his fard becomes four. The hadith 'al-imam ju'ila li-yu'tamma bihi' is the basis.
This applies even if he joins in the last part (e.g., tashahhud).
3
State the Ruling for After Time
After the prayer time has exited, the traveler's following is invalid: he does not ‘complete’ (la yu'ammih). The cause (time) is gone.
Exception: if the prayer is make-up (fa'itah) only for the imam and not the traveler, following is allowed.
4
Understand the Mixing of Fard and Nafl
If the traveler completes four, he intentionally mixes fard and nafl: the first sitting and recitation in the last two rak'ahs are fard for him but nafl for the resident imam. This is valid but requires careful intention.
Saying salam after two rak'ahs would oppose the imam and corrupt the prayer.
5
Identify the ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Imam Muhammad rejected contrary opinions, and the mufta bihi is that within time, four rak'ahs are wajib. Other schools may hold differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسافر أمه مقیم یتم في الوقت وبعده لا یؤمه

قوله: يتم؛ أي يجب على المقتدي المسافر الإتمام إذا كان الاقتداء في الوقت؛ لأن فرضه حينئذ يصير أربعاً بالتزامه متابعة الإمام، يؤخذ ذلك من حديث: «إنما جعل الإمام ليؤتم به»، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنه يتم وإن اقتدى به في جزء من صلاة، لو دخل معه في تشهده وَجَبَ عليه ما وجب على إمامه، كما حققه الإمام محمد له في كتاب «الحجج»، ورد على من قال بخلافه. [2] قوله: وبعده؛ أي لا يصح اقتداؤه بعد خروج الوقت؛ لعدم تغيره لانقضاء السبب، وهذا إذا كانت فائتة في حق الإمام والمأموم كليهما، فلو في حق الإمام يصح، كما لو اقتدى حنفي بشافعي في الظهر، وبمن يرى قولهما بعده المثل قبل المثلين، كما في «السراج الوهاج». قال في «البحر» : هو قيد حسن، لكنَّ الأولى اشتراط كونها فائتة في حق المأموم فقط، سواء فاتت الإمام أو لا، كمن صلى ركعة من الظهر فخرج الوقت فاقتدى به مسافر، فإنَّها فائتة في حق المسافر لا المقيم. [3] قوله: لا يتغير فرضُه؛ أي المسافر، فإن سلَّمَ على رأس الرَّكعتين كان مخالفاً لإمامه، وهو مفسد، وإن أتم أربعاً خلط نفلاً بفرض قصداً، فإنَّ القعدة الأولى فرض في حقه، نفل في حق الإمام، وكذلك القراءة في الأخريين، فيكون اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل في حق القعدة إن اقتدى به في أوَّلِ الصَّلاة أو القراءة إن اقتدى به في الشفع الثاني، كذا في «العناية».
A traveler whose imam is a resident completes (the prayer) within the time, but after it he does not follow him. He said: 'completes' means it is obligatory upon the follower traveler to complete if the following is within the time, because his obligation then becomes four by his commitment to follow the imam. This is taken from the hadith: 'The imam is only appointed to be followed.' The absolute expression indicates that he completes even if he follows him in a part of the prayer; if he enters with him in his tashahhud, what is obligatory on his imam becomes obligatory on him, as verified by Imam Muhammad in the book 'al-Hujaj', and he refuted those who said otherwise. [2] He said: 'after it' means his following is not valid after the exit of the time, due to the non-change because of the cessation of the cause. This is if the prayer is missed (fa'itah) for both the imam and the follower. But if it is missed only for the imam, it is valid, as when a Hanafi follows a Shafi'i in Zuhr, and one who holds their view (that after the time, the like before the likes) as in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj'. He said in 'al-Bahr': This is a good restriction, but the first is the condition that it is missed only for the follower, whether it is missed for the imam or not, like one who prayed one rak'ah of Zuhr then the time exited, and a traveler followed him; then it is missed for the traveler, not the resident. [3] He said: 'his obligation does not change' means the traveler's; if he says salam at the end of two rak'ahs, he opposes his imam which is corrupting; and if he completes four, he mixes nafl with fard intentionally, for the first sitting is fard for him but nafl for the imam, likewise the recitation in the last two; thus a muqtadi who is performing fard follows an imam performing nafl regarding the sitting if he follows him from the beginning of the prayer, or regarding the recitation if he follows him in the second pair of rak'ahs. This is in 'al-'Inayah'.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a traveler prays behind a resident imam. The matn states: 'A traveler whose imam is a resident completes the prayer within the time, but after it he does not follow him.' The commentary elaborates that 'completes' means it is wajib upon the traveler follower to complete four rak'ahs if he joins the congregation within the prescribed time. The reason is that by choosing to follow a resident imam, his fard becomes four rak'ahs due to his commitment to follow the imam, based on the hadith 'The imam is only appointed to be followed.' This applies even if he joins the imam in the last part of the prayer, such as the tashahhud. Imam Muhammad confirmed this in his book 'al-Hujaj'. Then the commentary addresses 'after it' meaning after the time has exited: the traveler's following is not valid because the cause for change (the time) has ended. However, there is a condition: this applies if the prayer is a make-up (fa'itah) for both the imam and the follower. If the prayer is missed only for the imam, the following is valid, as when a Hanafi follows a Shafi'i in Zuhr after the time. Further, the commentary mentions a refinement from 'al-Bahr' that it is better to condition that the prayer be missed only for the follower, regardless of the imam. Finally, it explains that if the traveler completes four rak'ahs, he must not say salam after two, as that would oppose the imam and corrupt the prayer. If he does complete four, he mixes nafl with fard, as the first sitting and recitation in the last two rak'ahs are fard for him but nafl for the resident imam. Thus, in such a case, the follower is effectively performing fard while the imam is performing nafl, which is valid but with specific considerations.

Key Terms
مقتدي (muqtadi): A follower in congregational prayer; the one praying behind the imam.
تمام (itmam): Completing the prayer to its full number of rak'ahs (four for Zuhr, etc.).
اقتداء (iqtida'): The act of following the imam in congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler joins a congregation led by a resident imam for Zuhr prayer within the time, what must he do?
A) He prays two rak'ahs and leaves.
B) He prays four rak'ahs, completing the prayer with the imam.
C) He prays two rak'ahs and then leaves after the imam's second rak'ah.
D) He does not join the congregation at all.

Key Takeaway
When a traveler follows a resident imam within the prayer time, he must complete four rak'ahs; after the time, he may only follow if the prayer is a make-up for the imam.

Traveler Imam Leading Residents
Ruling with proof and commentary on traveler imam leading residents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a traveler imam leading resident followers, explain the recommendation to announce 'Complete your prayer', and identify the consequence if the traveler completes four rak'ahs.

Core Idea
A traveler imam shortens his prayer; residents must complete theirs – and the imam should remind them.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the scenario where a traveler (musafir) leads residents (muqimin) in prayer. The traveler shortens his fard to two rak'ahs, whereas the followers are obligated to pray four. The Prophet's practice during the Conquest of Mecca provides the basis for a recommended announcement: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' We will analyze the ruling, its proof, and the important consequence if the traveler inadvertently completes the full prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The traveler shortens his prayer (qasr) to two rak'ahs, while the resident followers must complete four rak'ahs. This is established by the Prophet's sunnah.
The traveler's shortening is wajib for 4-rak'ah prayers; the resident's completion is fard.
2
Examine the recommendation
The imam says 'أتموا صلاتكم فإني مسافر' (Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler) as a recommendation (nadb), not an obligation. This is based on the Prophet's practice.
The word 'ندباً' means istihbab (preferred).
3
Investigate the dalil
The hadith of the Prophet during the Conquest of Mecca: he prayed two rak'ahs and told the Meccans to complete their prayer. This is the proof for the recommendation.
The hadith is mursal? Actually narrated by several sources; it is zanni al-thubut but accepted.
4
Understand the invalidation
If the traveler imam completes four rak'ahs (i.e., prays full), the prayer of the resident follower becomes invalid because the follower would be an obligated person (muftarid) following the imam's nafl in the last two rak'ahs.
This is a key consequence: the imam must not complete, otherwise the follower's prayer is fasid.
5
Note the condition of informing
If the imam informed the followers of his state before starting the prayer, no further announcement is needed. However, if a latecomer does not know, the imam should inform again. This shows that knowledge of the imam's state is not a condition for the validity of the prayer at the start.
The text implies it is better to inform, but not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عكسه قصر المسافر، وأتم المقيم، ويقول ندباً : أتموا صلاتكم، فإني مسافر. ويُبطل الوطن الأصلي مثلُهُ لا السفر، ووطن الإقامة مثله ، والسفر والأصلي) : الوطن الأصلي : هو المسكن. ووطنُ الإقامة : موضع نوى أن يستقر فيه خمسة عشر يوماً ، أو أكثر من غير أن يتخذه مسكناً. [١] قوله : قصر المسافر ؛ فإن أتم بالمقتدي المقيم، وتابع فسدت صلاة المقتدي للزوم اقتداء المفترض بالمتنقل في الأخريين، صرّح به الشرنبلالي في رسالته في «المسائل الإثني عشر». [۲] قوله : ندباً ؛ بالفتح : أي استحباباً ، لما روي أنه كان يصلي بهم ركعتين في غزوة فتح مكة بمكة، ويقول : يا أهل مكة أتموا صلاتكم فإِنَّا قوم سفر) بالفتح : جمع مسافر، أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود والطبراني، وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهم. وهذا إذا لم يخبر عن حاله عند ابتداء الصَّلاة، فإن أخبرهم به قبله لا يحتاج إلى الإعلام ثانياً إلا إذا دخل معه في أثناء الصَّلاة مقيم لم يعلم بحاله، ودلّت المسألة على أنه لا يشترط علم المقتدي حالَ الإمام . عند ابتداء صلاته. [۳] قوله : الوطن الأصلي... الخ ؛ قال في «الغنية»: «قالوا : الأوطان ثلاثة ؛ وطن أصلي، ووطن إقامة، ووطن سفر. فالأصلي : هو مولد الإنسان أو موضع تأهل به وقصد التعيش به لا الارتحال عنه، أما لو كان له أبوان ببلد غير مولده وهو بالغ، ولم يتأهل به، فليس ذلك وطناً له، وفي «المبسوط»: هو الذي نشأ فيه، أو توطن فيه ، أو تأهل فيه. فقوله : أو توطن فيه ؛ يتناولُ ما عزم القرار فيه، وإن لم يتأهل، فعلى هذا لو عزم من له أبوان في بلدٍ على القرار فيه وترك الوطن الذي قبله يكون وطنا له، ولو تزوج المسافر ببلد ولم ينو الإقامة، فقيل : لا يصير مقيماً ، وقيل : يصير مقيماً، وهو الأوجه.
And in its opposite: the traveler shortens, and the resident completes, and he says as a recommendation: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' And the original homeland nullifies like it; not travel; and the homeland of residence like it; and travel and the original. The original homeland is the dwelling. The homeland of residence is a place he intends to settle in for fifteen days or more without making it a dwelling. [1] His saying: 'the traveler shortens' – then if he completes with the resident follower, and he follows, the prayer of the follower becomes invalid due to the necessity of the obligated one following the one performing nafl in the last two rak'ahs. Al-Shurunbulali stated this in his treatise 'Al-Masa'il al-Ithna 'Ashar'. [2] His saying: 'as a recommendation' (nadban, with fathah) means as a preferred act, based on what is narrated that he (the Prophet) prayed two rak'ahs with them in the conquest of Mecca, and said: 'O people of Mecca, complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people.' (with fathah: plural of traveler) – narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others. This is if he did not inform them of his state at the beginning of the prayer. If he informed them before, he does not need to inform again unless a resident who did not know his state joins during the prayer. The issue indicates that it is not required for the follower to know the state of the imam at the start of his prayer. [3] His saying: 'The original homeland... etc.' He said in 'Al-Ghunyah': 'They said: the homelands are three: original homeland, homeland of residence, and homeland of travel. The original homeland is a person's birthplace, or a place where he has settled and intends to live permanently without leaving. As for if he has parents in a country other than his birthplace and he is an adult and has not settled there, that is not his homeland.' In 'Al-Mabsut': it is where he grew up, or settled, or established a household. His saying: 'or settled' includes the intention to stay, even without establishing a household. So on this basis, if someone with parents in a country intends to stay there and leaves his previous homeland, it becomes a homeland. If a traveler marries in a country without intending to stay, it is said he does not become a resident; and it is said he does become a resident, and that is the more correct view.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage discusses the reverse scenario where a traveler acts as imam for residents. The traveler shortens his prayer to two rak'ahs, but he must tell the resident followers to complete their prayer to four rak'ahs, because they are obligated to pray four. This announcement is recommended (nadb) based on the practice of the Prophet during the Conquest of Mecca. The commentary also notes that if the traveler completes four rak'ahs with the residents, the follower's prayer becomes invalid, because the follower is obligated to pray behind a traveler who only prays two as fard; completing four makes the traveler's last two rak'ahs nafl, and it is invalid for a muftarid (obligated person) to follow a mutanafifil (nafl prayer) in those rak'ahs. The second part defines the three homelands of Hanafi fiqh: the original homeland (watan asli) is where a person was born or has settled permanently; the homeland of residence (watan iqamah) is a place where one intends to stay fifteen days or more without making it a permanent dwelling; the homeland of travel (watan safar) begins when one leaves his residential homeland with the intention of traveling. These homelands affect when a traveler becomes a resident again and thus must pray full prayers. The commentary from 'Al-Ghunyah' expands on the definition of original homeland, including conditions of intent to stay. A point of ikhtilaf is whether marriage in a country without intention of stay makes one a resident; the more correct view is that it does.

Key Terms
مسافر (musafir): Traveler: a person who undertakes a journey of three days (or 48 miles) with the intention of traveling, thus allowed to shorten prayers.
مقيم (muqim): Resident: a person who is not a traveler, either because they are in their homeland or intend to stay fifteen days or more in a place.
ندب (nadb): Recommendation: an act that is praised but not obligatory; its omission is not sinful.
إتمام (itmam): Completion: to pray a prayer in its full form (e.g., four rak'ahs for Dhuhr) as opposed to qasr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a traveler imam say to resident followers?
A) 'Pray behind me as I pray two rak'ahs'
B) 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler'
C) 'Shorten your prayer like me'
D) Nothing; they should follow silently

Key Takeaway
The traveler imam shortens his prayer and recommends residents to complete, based on the Prophet's Sunnah; failure to shorten invalidates the followers' prayer.
