Definitions of Homelands (Watan Asli, Watan Iqamah, Watan Safar)
Commentary defining the three homelands and a disputed case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three types of homelands in Hanafi fiqh, distinguish between them, and apply the ruling of watan asli on traveler status.

Core Idea
Your homeland type determines whether you are a traveler or resident for prayer.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of homelands: watan asli (original homeland), watan iqamah (residence homeland), and watan safar (travel homeland). These definitions are crucial because entering an original homeland nullifies one's traveler status and requires full prayers, while entering a residence homeland does not nullify unless one intends to stay fifteen days or more. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah provides the definitions and also discusses a disputed case: does marriage in a country without intention to stay make one a resident? The more correct view is that it does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three homelands
The passage lists three: watan asli (original), watan iqamah (residence), watan safar (travel). Understand each definition.
Watan asli is permanent dwelling or birthplace with intent to stay indefinitely.
2
Define watan asli
Watan asli is the place where a person was born, or where they have settled permanently with no intention to leave. It includes growing up, settling, or establishing a household. The passage from Al-Ghunyah and Al-Mabsut provides details.
Having parents in a country does not automatically make it watan asli if one has not settled there.
3
Define watan iqamah
Watan iqamah is a place where one intends to stay for fifteen days or more without making it a permanent dwelling. This is a temporary residence that does not nullify a previous watan asli.
Fewer than fifteen days means one is still a traveler.
4
Define watan safar
Watan safar is any place that is neither watan asli nor watan iqamah. It is where one passes through or stays briefly below fifteen days. The traveler status remains until entering a watan asli or intending watan iqamah.
Often referred to as the state of being a musafir.
5
Analyze the disputed case: marriage without intention
If a traveler marries in a country but does not intend to stay fifteen days, does he become a resident? One view says no, the more correct (الأوجه) view says yes, because marriage implies stability.
This affects whether he must pray full or shortened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عكسه قصر المسافر، وأتم المقيم، ويقول ندباً : أتموا صلاتكم، فإني مسافر. ويُبطل الوطن الأصلي مثلُهُ لا السفر، ووطن الإقامة مثله ، والسفر والأصلي) : الوطن الأصلي : هو المسكن. ووطنُ الإقامة : موضع نوى أن يستقر فيه خمسة عشر يوماً ، أو أكثر من غير أن يتخذه مسكناً. [١] قوله : قصر المسافر ؛ فإن أتم بالمقتدي المقيم، وتابع فسدت صلاة المقتدي للزوم اقتداء المفترض بالمتنقل في الأخريين، صرّح به الشرنبلالي في رسالته في «المسائل الإثني عشر». [۲] قوله : ندباً ؛ بالفتح : أي استحباباً ، لما روي أنه كان يصلي بهم ركعتين في غزوة فتح مكة بمكة، ويقول : يا أهل مكة أتموا صلاتكم فإِنَّا قوم سفر) بالفتح : جمع مسافر، أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود والطبراني، وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهم. وهذا إذا لم يخبر عن حاله عند ابتداء الصَّلاة، فإن أخبرهم به قبله لا يحتاج إلى الإعلام ثانياً إلا إذا دخل معه في أثناء الصَّلاة مقيم لم يعلم بحاله، ودلّت المسألة على أنه لا يشترط علم المقتدي حالَ الإمام . عند ابتداء صلاته. [۳] قوله : الوطن الأصلي... الخ ؛ قال في «الغنية»: «قالوا : الأوطان ثلاثة ؛ وطن أصلي، ووطن إقامة، ووطن سفر. فالأصلي : هو مولد الإنسان أو موضع تأهل به وقصد التعيش به لا الارتحال عنه، أما لو كان له أبوان ببلد غير مولده وهو بالغ، ولم يتأهل به، فليس ذلك وطناً له، وفي «المبسوط»: هو الذي نشأ فيه، أو توطن فيه ، أو تأهل فيه. فقوله : أو توطن فيه ؛ يتناولُ ما عزم القرار فيه، وإن لم يتأهل، فعلى هذا لو عزم من له أبوان في بلدٍ على القرار فيه وترك الوطن الذي قبله يكون وطنا له، ولو تزوج المسافر ببلد ولم ينو الإقامة، فقيل : لا يصير مقيماً ، وقيل : يصير مقيماً، وهو الأوجه.
And in its opposite: the traveler shortens, and the resident completes, and he says as a recommendation: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' And the original homeland nullifies like it; not travel; and the homeland of residence like it; and travel and the original. The original homeland is the dwelling. The homeland of residence is a place he intends to settle in for fifteen days or more without making it a dwelling. [1] His saying: 'the traveler shortens' – then if he completes with the resident follower, and he follows, the prayer of the follower becomes invalid due to the necessity of the obligated one following the one performing nafl in the last two rak'ahs. Al-Shurunbulali stated this in his treatise 'Al-Masa'il al-Ithna 'Ashar'. [2] His saying: 'as a recommendation' (nadban, with fathah) means as a preferred act, based on what is narrated that he (the Prophet) prayed two rak'ahs with them in the conquest of Mecca, and said: 'O people of Mecca, complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people.' (with fathah: plural of traveler) – narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others. This is if he did not inform them of his state at the beginning of the prayer. If he informed them before, he does not need to inform again unless a resident who did not know his state joins during the prayer. The issue indicates that it is not required for the follower to know the state of the imam at the start of his prayer. [3] His saying: 'The original homeland... etc.' He said in 'Al-Ghunyah': 'They said: the homelands are three: original homeland, homeland of residence, and homeland of travel. The original homeland is a person's birthplace, or a place where he has settled and intends to live permanently without leaving. As for if he has parents in a country other than his birthplace and he is an adult and has not settled there, that is not his homeland.' In 'Al-Mabsut': it is where he grew up, or settled, or established a household. His saying: 'or settled' includes the intention to stay, even without establishing a household. So on this basis, if someone with parents in a country intends to stay there and leaves his previous homeland, it becomes a homeland. If a traveler marries in a country without intending to stay, it is said he does not become a resident; and it is said he does become a resident, and that is the more correct view.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage discusses the reverse scenario where a traveler acts as imam for residents. The traveler shortens his prayer to two rak'ahs, but he must tell the resident followers to complete their prayer to four rak'ahs, because they are obligated to pray four. This announcement is recommended (nadb) based on the practice of the Prophet during the Conquest of Mecca. The commentary also notes that if the traveler completes four rak'ahs with the residents, the follower's prayer becomes invalid, because the follower is obligated to pray behind a traveler who only prays two as fard; completing four makes the traveler's last two rak'ahs nafl, and it is invalid for a muftarid (obligated person) to follow a mutanafifil (nafl prayer) in those rak'ahs. The second part defines the three homelands of Hanafi fiqh: the original homeland (watan asli) is where a person was born or has settled permanently; the homeland of residence (watan iqamah) is a place where one intends to stay fifteen days or more without making it a permanent dwelling; the homeland of travel (watan safar) begins when one leaves his residential homeland with the intention of traveling. These homelands affect when a traveler becomes a resident again and thus must pray full prayers. The commentary from 'Al-Ghunyah' expands on the definition of original homeland, including conditions of intent to stay. A point of ikhtilaf is whether marriage in a country without intention of stay makes one a resident; the more correct view is that it does.

Key Terms
وطن أصلي (watan asli): Original homeland: the place where a person was born, or has settled permanently with intent to live there indefinitely.
وطن إقامة (watan iqamah): Homeland of residence: a place where one intends to stay for fifteen days or more without making it a permanent dwelling.
وطن سفر (watan safar): Homeland of travel: any place that is not an original or residence homeland, where the traveler intends to stay less than fifteen days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes watan asli (original homeland) according to the passage?
A) The place where a person's parents live.
B) The place where a person has permanently settled with no intention to leave.
C) The place where a person intends to stay for fifteen days or more.
D) The place where a person is currently staying.

Key Takeaway
The three homelands determine traveler status: watan asli nullifies travel, watan iqamah requires 15 days, and watan safar is temporary.

Traveler Leading Resident: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on traveler imam leading resident and command to complete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a traveler leads a resident in prayer and explain the proof from the Sunnah, identifying the invalidity condition.

Core Idea
The traveler shortens; the resident must complete four rak'ahs — following the traveler's qasr invalidates the resident's prayer.

When a traveler leads a resident congregation, a specialized ruling applies: the traveler prays two rak'ahs (qasr) while each resident must complete four on their own after the imam's tasleem. The imam is recommended to announce his travel status. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof from the Prophet's practice, and the critical invalidity condition if the resident mistakenly ends with the imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled: a traveler leading a resident in prayer. The ruling: traveler shortens (qasr), resident completes (atamma).
The resident's completion is obligatory (wajib).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith from the Conquest of Mecca where the Prophet prayed two rak'ahs and told the Meccans to complete.
Hadith is ahad, transmission is zanni, but meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action shows qasr is allowed when leading residents; his command 'أتموا' indicates recommendation (nadb) for him to announce, not obligation.
Hanafi usul: al-amr al-sadir min al-Nabi fi ghayr al-wajib yufidu al-nadb.
4
Identify the invalidity condition
If the resident follower completes with the traveler (i.e., follows the imam's tasleem after two rak'ahs), his prayer becomes invalid because the last two rak'ahs would be performed as a muqtadi behind someone performing nafl.
This is based on al-Shurunbulali's clarification.
5
Note the practical implications
The imam should announce his travel if not known; if announced at start, no need to repeat unless a new person joins later. Knowledge of imam's state is not a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة صلاة مسافر
وفي عكسه قصر المسافر، وأتم المقيم، ويقول ندباً : أتموا صلاتكم، فإني مسافر
[قوله : قصر المسافر؛ فإن أتم بالمقتدي المقيم، وتابع فسدت صلاة المقتدي للزوم اقتداء المفترض بالمتنقل في الأخريين، صرّح به الشرنبلالي في رسالته في «المسائل الإثني عشر». 
قوله : ندباً ؛ بالفتح : أي استحباباً ، لما روي أنه كان يصلي بهم ركعتين في غزوة فتح مكة بمكة، ويقول : يا أهل مكة أتموا صلاتكم فإِنَّا قوم سفر) بالفتح : جمع مسافر، أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود والطبراني، وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهم.
وهذا إذا لم يخبر عن حاله عند ابتداء الصَّلاة، فإن أخبرهم به قبله لا يحتاج إلى الإعلام ثانياً إلا إذا دخل معه في أثناء الصَّلاة مقيم لم يعلم بحاله، ودلّت المسألة على أنه لا يشترط علم المقتدي حالَ الإمام . عند ابتداء صلاته.]
Book of Prayer – Prayer of a Traveler. And in its opposite: the traveler shortens, and the resident completes, and he says, as a recommendation: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' [Commentary: His statement: 'the traveler shortens' – for if he completes with a resident follower, and the follower follows, the follower's prayer becomes invalid due to the necessary following of one obligated by the one performing a supererogatory act in the last two rak'ahs, as stated by al-Shurunbulali in his treatise on the twelve issues. His statement: 'recommendation' – meaning desirability, as it is narrated that he prayed two rak'ahs with them in the conquest of Mecca, saying: 'O people of Mecca, complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people.' This is if he did not inform them of his state at the beginning of the prayer; if he informed them before, he does not need to inform again unless a resident who did not know his state joins during the prayer. The issue indicates that it is not a condition for the follower to know the imam's state at the beginning of his prayer.]
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when a traveler leads a resident in prayer. The traveler prays two rak'ahs (qasr) while the resident must complete four. The imam is recommended to announce 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler' so that followers know. The commentary clarifies a critical point: if the resident follower mistakenly completes with the traveler (i.e., prays two rak'ahs following the traveler's qasr and then adds two on his own in a way that follows the imam in the extra rak'ahs), his prayer is invalid because the last two rak'ahs would be performed as a muqtadi behind someone who is performing nafl (since the traveler's prayer ends after two). The hadith evidence from the Conquest of Mecca supports the practice. Additionally, if the traveler informed his followers before prayer, no repetition is needed unless a new resident joins during the prayer who was unaware. This shows that knowing the imam's intention is not a condition at the start.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayer to two rak'ahs while traveling.
أتم (atamma): To complete the prayer fully (four rak'ahs) as normally required for a resident.
مقتدي (muqtadi): The person following the imam in congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Invalid, because he omitted the wajib completion.
C) Valid only if he was unaware of the imam's travel.
D) Invalid only if he intended to shorten.

Key Takeaway
A traveler leading residents must pray qasr, residents must complete four rak'ahs; the imam is recommended to announce his travel, and the resident's prayer is invalid if he ends with the imam.

Types of Homeland and Their Invalidation Rules
Definition of three homelands and their invalidation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three types of homeland (watan) in Hanafi fiqh and apply their invalidation rules to determine residency status for prayer.

Core Idea
An original homeland is only lost by adopting another original; a residence homeland is lost by travel or another residence.

In Hanafi fiqh, a person's prayer length depends on whether they are a resident or traveler. This distinction hinges on the concept of 'homeland' (watan). The classical scholars identified three types of homeland, each with its own rules of invalidation. This lesson unpacks those definitions and the hierarchy of invalidation, using the detailed commentary in the source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three homelands
Name the three types: watan asli (original), watan iqama (residence), watan safar (travel). Note their defining characteristics.
2
Define each homeland with its conditions
For watan asli: permanent settlement, birth, or family with intention of permanence. For watan iqama: intention to stay 15+ days without making it a permanent dwelling. For watan safar: travel without such intention.
The condition of 15 days is specific to Hanafi; other schools differ.
3
Understand the invalidation hierarchy
Watan asli is invalidated only by another watan asli. Watan iqama is invalidated by travel, another watan iqama, or returning to watan asli.
The phrase 'like for like' is key: original invalidates original, residence invalidates residence.
4
Apply to concrete examples
Work through the examples in the commentary: Prophet in Mecca, the Dehlawi traveler, the person with family in two cities. Determine whether they are residents or travelers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُبطل الوطن الأصلي مثلُهُ لا السفر، ووطن الإقامة مثله ، والسفر والأصلي
الوطن الأصلي : هو المسكن. ووطنُ الإقامة : موضع نوى أن يستقر فيه خمسة عشر يوماً ، أو أكثر من غير أن يتخذه مسكناً.
قوله : الوطن الأصلي... الخ ؛ قال في «الغنية»: «قالوا : الأوطان ثلاثة ؛ وطن أصلي، ووطن إقامة، ووطن سفر. فالأصلي : هو مولد الإنسان أو موضع تأهل به وقصد التعيش به لا الارتحال عنه، أما لو كان له أبوان ببلد غير مولده وهو بالغ، ولم يتأهل به، فليس ذلك وطناً له، وفي «المبسوط»: هو الذي نشأ فيه، أو توطن فيه ، أو تأهل فيه. فقوله : أو توطن فيه ؛ يتناولُ ما عزم القرار فيه، وإن لم يتأهل، فعلى هذا لو عزم من له أبوان في بلدٍ على القرار فيه وترك الوطن الذي قبله يكون وطنا له، ولو تزوج المسافر ببلد ولم ينو الإقامة، فقيل : لا يصير مقيماً ، وقيل : يصير مقيماً، وهو الأوجه.
فإن كان للإنسان وطن أصلي، ثُمَّ اتخذ موضعاً آخر وطناً أصلياً سواء كان بينهما مدة السفر، أو لم يكن ، يُبطل الوطن الأصلي الْأَوَّل ، حَتَّى لِو دَخَلَهُ لَا يصير مقيماً إلا بنية الإقامة، لكن لا يَبْطُلُ الأصلي بالسفر ، حتّى لو قَدِمَ المسافر الوطن الأصلي يصير مقيماً بمجرد الدخول. وأما وطن الإقامة، فإنَّه يَبْطُلُ بوطن الإقامة ، فإنه إذا كان له وطن إقامة ، ثم اتخذ موضعاً آخر وطن إقامته ، وليس بينهما مدة سفر، لم يبق الموضع الأَوَّلُ وطن الإقامة حتّى لو دخله لا يصير مقيما إلا بالنية ، وكذا إن سافر عنه ، إن انتقل إلى وطنه الأصلي. ولو كان له أهل ببلدتين فأيهما دخلها صار مقيماً ، وإن ماتت زوجته في أحدهما وبقي له دور وعقار فيها ، قيل : لا يبقى وطناً له ، وقيل : يبقى. ووطن الإقامة: ما ينوي فيه الإقامة خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً، ولم يكن مولده، ولا له أهل به». انتهى.
قوله : يبطل الوطن الأصلي ؛ يدلُّ عليه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه لما دخلوا مكة في غزوة الفتح وحجة الوداع قصروا فيها ، مع أنها كانت مولدهم ومسكنهم، وما ذلك إلا لبطلان وطنيّته باتخاذ المدينة وطناً بالهجرة.
قوله : بالسفر ؛ بأن يخرج منه قاصداً مكاناً بينه وبينه مدة السفر ؛ لأنَّ الشَّيء إنّما يبطل بما يساويه أو بشيء فوقه لا بما هو دونه.
قوله : فإنه...الخ ؛ صورته : دهلوي دخل بلدة لكهنؤ، فأقام بها خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً، وأتم الصَّلاة، ثم خرج إلى إله آباد، ونوى إقامة خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً هناك، ثم خرج منه يريد وطنه الأصلي فمرّ بلكهنؤ، ولم ينو فيه الإقامة، لا يتم صلاته ؛ لأنه لم يكن وطناً أصلياً له، بل وطن إقامة وبطل ذلك بوطن آخر.
قوله : وليس بينهما مدة السفر ؛ هذا قيد اتفاقي ؛ لأنَّ وطن الإقامة يبطل بمثله سواء كان بينهما مقدار السفرِ أو لا ، وإنما ذكره ليتّضح مثال بطلان وطن الإقامة بمثله ، فإنّه إذا كانت بينهما مسيرة سفر يبطل وطنُ الإقامة بنفس السفر منه أيضاً.
قوله : وكذا إن سافر ؛ أي يبطل وطن الإقامة إذا سافر منه ؛ لأنه إنما صار وطناً بإقامته ، والسفر ضده فتبطل بوروده.
قوله : وكذا ؛ أي يبطل وطنُ الإقامة بانتقاله إلى وطنه الأصلي، حتى لو دخل
And the original homeland is invalidated by its like, not by travel; and the residence homeland is invalidated by its like, and travel and the original [homeland]. The original homeland: it is the dwelling. The residence homeland: a place where one intends to settle for fifteen days or more without making it a dwelling. [Commentary] ... The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) and his Companions shortened in Mecca during the Conquest and Farewell Pilgrimage even though it was their birthplace and dwelling; that was only due to the invalidation of its being a homeland by taking Medina as a homeland through migration. ...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of homeland in Hanafi fiqh: original homeland (watan asli), residence homeland (watan iqama), and travel homeland (watan safar). The original homeland is where one was born or has settled with family and intends permanent residence. It is only invalidated by taking another original homeland, not by travel or by taking a residence homeland. The residence homeland is a place where one intends to stay fifteen days or more but not as a permanent home; it is invalidated by another residence homeland or by traveling away from it. The commentary gives examples: if someone has a residence homeland in Lucknow and then takes another in Allahabad, the first is invalidated. The Prophet's example of shortening in Mecca after migrating to Medina shows that the original homeland is lost by adopting a new one. A key point: returning to an original homeland immediately makes one a resident; no additional intention needed. For a residence homeland, one must intend fifteen days to become a resident.

Key Terms
وطن أصلي (waṭan aṣlī): Permanent homeland where one was born, resides with family, or intends to settle permanently.
وطن إقامة (waṭan iqāma): Place where one intends to stay fifteen days or more, without making it a permanent dwelling.
وطن سفر (waṭan safar): Place through which one travels without intending residence (i.e., less than 15 days stay).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What invalidates a watan iqama according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only another watan iqama.
B) Travel, another watan iqama, or returning to watan asli.
C) Only travel.
D) Only returning to watan asli.

Key Takeaway
An original homeland is only lost by adopting another original; a residence homeland is lost by travel, another residence, or returning to the original.

Rulings on Original and Stay Residence for the Traveler
Defines watan types and proves invalidation via Prophetic action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of original home and stay residence, apply the rules of invalidation, and explain the prophetic proof supporting the invalidation of original home.

Core Idea
Adopting a new original home invalidates the previous one; travel alone does not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the key rulings on residence types that affect a traveler's prayer. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly determining when to shorten prayers (qasr) and when to pray fully. The commentary builds on Al-Wiqaya, providing definitions, rules, and a proof from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of residence
Read the definitions of 'original home' (watan asli) and 'stay residence' (watan iqama). Understand that the original home is where one was born or has family, while stay residence is a temporary place for fifteen days or more.
The original home is only invalidated by another original home, not by travel.
2
Examine the invalidation rules
Identify what invalidates each type: original home is invalidated by adopting another original home; stay residence is invalidated by another stay residence, by traveling away, or by returning to the original home.
Travel alone does not invalidate the original home, but it does invalidate stay residence.
3
Analyze the prophetic proof
The text cites the Prophet and Companions performing qasr in Mecca during the Conquest and Farewell Pilgrimage, even though Mecca was their birthplace. This proves that adopting Medina as a new home invalidated Mecca's status as original home.
The hadith is mutawatir in transmission but zanni in indication (dalala), as the specific legal ruling is derived through inference.
4
Work through the given examples
Apply the rules to the example of a Dehlavi who stays in Lucknow, then moves to Allahabad, and passes through Lucknow again. Determine when he must shorten his prayer.
The outcome depends on whether the location remains a stay residence or has been invalidated.
5
Consider the ikhtilaf on property
Note the dispute: if one's wife dies and he retains property, does it remain a home? The text presents two views; the relied-upon position is that property alone is insufficient.
This dispute affects how one determines whether a place is still an original home.

فإن كان للإنسان وطن أصلي، ثُمَّ اتخذ موضعاً آخر وطناً أصلياً سواء كان بينهما مدة السفر، أو لم يكن، يُبطل الوطن الأصلي الأول، حتى لو دخل لا يصير مقيماً إلا بنية الإقامة، لكن لا يَبْطُلُ الأصلي بالسفر، حتى لو قَدِمَ المسافر الوطن الأصلي يصير مقيماً بمجرد الدخول. وأما وطن الإقامة، فإنَّه يَبْطُلُ بوطن الإقامة، فإنه إذا كان له وطن إقامة، ثم اتخذ موضعاً آخر وطن إقامته، وليس بينهما مدة سفر، لم يبق الموضعُ الأول وطن الإقامة حتى لو دخله لا يصير مقيما إلا بالنية، وكذا إن سافر عنه، إن انتقل إلى وطنه الأصلي. ولو كان له أهل ببلدتين فأيهما دخلها صار مقيماً، وإن ماتت زوجته في أحدهما وكذا وبقي له دور وعقار فيها، قيل: لا يبقى وطناً له، وقيل: يبقى. ووطن الإقامة: ما ينوي فيه الإقامة خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً، ولم يكن مولده، ولا له أهل به». انتهى. [١]قوله: يبطل الوطن الأصلي؛ يدلُّ عليه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه لما دخلوا مكة في غزوة الفتح وحجة الوداع قصروا فيها، مع أنها كانت مولدهم ومسكنهم، وما ذلك إلا لبطلان وطنيّته باتخاذ المدينة وطناً بالهجرة. [٢]قوله: بالسفر؛ بأن يخرج منه قاصداً مكاناً بينه وبينه مدة السفر؛ لأنَّ الشَّيء إنّما يبطل بما يساويه أو بشيء فوقه لا بما هو دونه. [٣]قوله: فإنه...الخ؛ صورته: دهلوي دخل بلدة لكهنؤ، فأقام بها خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً، وأتم الصَّلاة، ثم خرج إلى إله آباد، ونوى إقامة خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً هناك، ثم خرج منه يريد وطنه الأصلي فمرّ بلكهنؤ، ولم ينو فيه الإقامة، لا يتم صلاته؛ لأنه لم يكن وطناً أصلياً له، بل وطن إقامة وبطل ذلك بوطن آخر. [٤]قوله: وليس بينهما مدة السفر؛ هذا قيد اتفاقي؛ لأنَّ وطن الإقامة يبطل بمثله سواء كان بينهما مقدار السفرِ أو لا، وإنما ذكره ليتّضح مثال بطلان وطن الإقامة بمثله، فإنّه إذا كانت بينهما مسيرة سفر يبطل وطنُ الإقامة بنفس السفر منه أيضاً. [0]قوله: وكذا إن سافر؛ أي يبطل وطن الإقامة إذا سافر منه؛ لأنه إنما صار وطناً بإقامته، والسفر ضده فتبطل بوروده. [٦]قوله: وكذا؛ أي يبطل وطنُ الإقامة بانتقاله إلى وطنه الأصلي، حتى لو دخل فيه ثانياً يقصر ما لم ينو الإقامة ثانياً.
If a person has an original home, then adopts another location as an original home, whether the distance of travel exists between them or not, it invalidates the first original home, so that even if he enters it he does not become a resident except with the intention of residence. But the original home is not invalidated by travel, so if a traveler arrives at his original home, he becomes a resident merely by entering. As for the stay residence, it is invalidated by another stay residence. So if he has a stay residence, then adopts another location as his stay residence, and the distance of travel is not between them, the first location does not remain a stay residence so that even if he enters it he does not become a resident except by intention. Likewise, if he travels from it, and if he moves to his original home. And if he has family in two towns, whichever he enters he becomes a resident, and if his wife dies in one of them and he still has houses and property in it, it is said: it does not remain a home for him, and it is said: it remains. The stay residence: that in which he intends residence for fifteen days or more, and it is not his birthplace nor does he have family there. End. [1] His saying: it invalidates the original home; the evidence for it is that the Prophet (pbuh) and his Companions when they entered Mecca during the Conquest and the Farewell Pilgrimage, they performed qasr in it, even though it was their birthplace and residence, and that was only due to the invalidation of its being a home by adopting Medina as a home through emigration. [2] His saying: through travel; i.e., that he leaves it intending a place between which and it is the distance of travel; because a thing is only invalidated by what is equal to it or something above it, not by what is below it. [3] His saying: then...etc.; its example: A Dehlavi entered the city of Lucknow and stayed there fifteen days or more and performed complete prayer, then went to Allahabad and intended residence of fifteen days or more there, then left it intending his original home and passed through Lucknow without intending residence there: he does not complete his prayer because it was not his original home but a stay residence, and that was invalidated by another. [4] His saying: and there is not between them the distance of travel; this is an accidental condition; because stay residence is invalidated by its equivalent whether the distance of travel exists between them or not, but he mentioned it to clarify the example of invalidation of stay residence by its equivalent, because if the distance of travel is between them, the stay residence is also invalidated by the travel itself. [0] His saying: likewise if he travels; i.e., stay residence is invalidated if he travels from it because it only became a home by his stay, and travel is its opposite so it is invalidated by its occurrence. [6] His saying: likewise; i.e., stay residence is invalidated by moving to his original home, so that even if he enters it a second time he performs qasr as long as he does not intend residence a second time.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on Al-Wiqaya, explaining the rulings of 'watan' (residence) and their effect on the traveler's prayer (qasr). The author first defines two types of residence: al-watan al-asli (original home) and watan al-iqama (stay residence). The original home is where a person was born or has family and intends to live permanently; it is only invalidated by adopting another original home, not by travel. The stay residence is a place where one intends to stay for fifteen days or more; it is invalidated by moving to another stay residence, by traveling away, or by returning to the original home. The author provides a proof for the original home invalidation from the Prophet's action during the conquest of Mecca and the Farewell Pilgrimage: even though Mecca was their birthplace, the companions performed qasr because Medina had become their original home through hijra. He then gives practical examples, such as a person from Delhi who stays in Lucknow, then moves to Allahabad, and upon passing through Lucknow without intending residence, must shorten his prayer there. The passage also addresses a dispute: if one has property in a place but no family, some say it remains a home, others that it does not. The relied-upon position is that property alone does not make a place an original home.

Key Terms
وطن أصلي (watan asli): The place where a person was born or has family and intends to live permanently; it is their original home.
وطن إقامة (watan iqama): A place where a person intends to stay for fifteen days or more, but where they have no family or birthplace.
مدة السفر (muddat al-safar): The distance that constitutes a legal travel (safar), typically a three-day journey (approximately 48 miles/77 km) by camel or foot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person has an original home in City A. He then adopts City B as a new original home. After that, he visits City A for a week. How should he pray?
A) He should perform qasr because City A is no longer his original home.
B) He should pray full prayers because City A was his original home.
C) He must intend residence for 15 days to avoid qasr.
D) He should pray qasr only if the distance between City A and City B is travel distance.

Key Takeaway
An original home is only invalidated by adopting another original home; stay residence is invalidated by travel, another stay residence, or return to original home.

Qada of Missed Prayers: The Original Time Controls
Matn and commentary on qada of prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a missed prayer is made up according to the ruling of the time it was missed and apply it to both travel and residence scenarios.

Core Idea
A missed prayer retains its original ruling; travel and residence do not change it.

When you miss a prayer, the way you make it up depends on when you missed it, not when you make it up. This principle is stated concisely in the matn: 'Travel and its opposite do not change the missed prayer.' The commentator expands with a clear analogy to a sick person's prayer. Let's unpack the rule and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states that travel and residence do not change the missed prayer. This means the ruling (qasr or complete) is fixed at the time the prayer became obligatory.
2
Apply the two scenarios
If a prayer is missed while traveling, it must be made up shortened even if made up in residence. If missed in residence, it must be made up complete even if made up while traveling.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogy)
The commentator compares to a sick person who cannot stand: if he misses a prayer during illness, he must make it up standing when healthy. The concession (sitting) was temporary; when gone, the original obligation returns. Similarly, travel does not create a permanent concession – the traveler’s prayer is inherently two rak'ahs.
This qiyas is jali (clear) because the 'illa is the removal of the concession.
4
Address the counterargument
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) argue that the reason for shortening (travel) is gone at the time of qada, so the full prayer should be offered. The Hanafi response: the traveler's prayer is not a concession but the original form for a traveler; the obligation was fixed as two rak'ahs, so it remains.
This illustrates the Hanafi view that qasr is not a rukhsah but a determined form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والسفر وضده لا يغيران الفائتة) : أي إذا قضى فائتةَ السَّفْرِ في الحضرِ يَقْصُرُ، وإن قضى فائتة الحضر في السفر يتم. والله أعلم . (1) قوله : أي إذا قضى.... الخ ؛ قال في «فتح القدير » : «لا يشكل على هذا المريض إذا فاتته صلاة في مرضه الذي لا يقدر فيه على القيام، فإنه يجب أن يقضيها في الصحة قائماً ؛ لأنَّ الوجوب بقيد القيام غير أنه رخص له أن يفعلها حالة العذر بقدر وسعه إذ ذاك، فحين لم يؤديها حالة العذرِ زالَ سبب الرخصة، فتعين الأصل، أما صلاة المسافر فليست إلا ركعتين».
And travel and its opposite do not change the missed prayer: i.e., if he makes up a missed prayer of travel while in residence, he shortens; and if he makes up a missed prayer of residence while traveling, he completes. And Allah knows best. (1) His saying: i.e., if he makes up... etc.; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'This is not problematic with the sick person if a prayer is missed during his illness in which he is unable to stand, for he must make it up while healthy standing; because the obligation is linked to standing, but he was given a concession to perform it during the excuse to the extent of his ability at that time. So when he did not perform it during the excuse, the reason for the concession is removed, so the original becomes determined. As for the traveler's prayer, it is only two rak'ahs.'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This short passage states a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: when making up a missed prayer (qada), the ruling of the time when the prayer was missed applies, not the ruling of the time when it is made up. The matn says: 'Travel and its opposite (i.e., residence) do not change the missed prayer.' The commentator explains: if someone misses a prayer while traveling, even if they make it up while in residence, they perform it shortened (qasr). Conversely, if they miss a prayer while in residence, even if they make it up while traveling, they perform it completely. The commentator from Fath al-Qadir provides an analogy with the sick person: if a sick person who cannot stand misses a prayer, he must make it up standing when healthy, because the concession (praying sitting) was only for the time of the excuse. Similarly, the traveler's prayer is inherently two rak'ahs; it is not a concession but the prescribed form. Therefore, the obligation remains the same in qada.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'itah): A missed prayer that is required to be made up (qada).
قضى (qaḍā): To make up or perform a missed prayer after its time has passed.
قصر (qaṣr): Shortening the prayer, i.e., performing four-rak'ah prayers as two rak'ahs during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person misses Zuhr prayer during a journey (safar) and later makes it up at home. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, how should he perform the qada?
A) 4 rak'ahs (complete)
B) 2 rak'ahs (shortened)
C) 2 rak'ahs only if he had intended to travel again
D) He may choose either option

Key Takeaway
When making up a missed prayer, always follow the ruling of the time it was missed, regardless of your current state.

Make-up Prayer Follows Original Obligation
Ruling with proof from Fath al-Qadir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the number of rak'ahs in a make-up prayer is determined by the original obligation, and distinguish it from the case of a sick person's concession.

Core Idea
The make-up prayer replicates the original obligation, not the current situation.

This lesson addresses a common confusion: when you miss a prayer while traveling and make it up at home, how many rak'ah should you pray? The Hanafi school has a clear rule based on the principle that the original obligation determines the qada. We will examine the passage and its proof, which contrasts with the case of a sick person whose concession is situational.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'Travel and its opposite do not change the make-up prayer.' This means the condition at the time of making up (residence or travel) does not affect the number of rak'ah.
The ruling is stated in a single declarative sentence.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The commentator explains: if you miss a travel prayer (2 rak'ah) and make it up in residence, you still shorten (2 rak'ah). If you miss a residence prayer (4 rak'ah) and make it up while traveling, you complete (4 rak'ah).
This is the core application of the rule.
3
Examine the proof from Fath al-Qadir
The passage quotes Fath al-Qadir, which contrasts the traveler's case with a sick person. The sick person's concession to pray sitting is only for the excuse; when he recovers, he must stand. For the traveler, the prayer itself is only 2 rak'ah - it is not a concession but an inherent obligation.
Thus, the traveler's short prayer is the original obligation, not a concession to be removed.
4
Distinguish the sick person's case
Understand why the sick person's qada changes: his original obligation was to stand, but he was excused; when the excuse ends, the original obligation returns. The traveler's original obligation is 2 rak'ah, so it persists.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(1) ١ ] قوله : أي إذا قضى.... الخ ؛ قال في «فتح القدير » : «لا يشكل على هذا المريض إذا فاتته صلاة في مرضه الذي لا يقدر فيه على القيام، فإنه يجب أن يقضيها في الصحة قائماً ؛ لأنَّ الوجوب بقيد القيام غير أنه رخص له أن يفعلها حالة العذر بقدر وسعه إذ ذاك، فحين لم يؤديها حالة العذرِ زالَ سبب الرخصة، فتعين الأصل، أما صلاة المسافر فليست إلا ركعتين». (۱) «فتح القدير»(٢ : ٤٦).
And travel and its opposite do not change the make-up prayer: That is, if one makes up a missed prayer of travel in residence, he shortens; and if he makes up a missed prayer of residence in travel, he completes. And Allah knows best. In it, second: he shortens as long as he does not intend residence a second time. (1) His saying: 'That is, if one makes up...' etc.; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'This is not problematic for the sick person: if a prayer is missed during his illness in which he is unable to stand, then he must make it up while healthy standing; because the obligation is qualified by standing, but he was granted a concession to perform it during the excuse to the extent of his ability at that time. So when he did not perform it during the excuse, the reason for the concession ceases, so the original is determined. As for the traveler's prayer, it is only two rak'ahs.' (from Fath al-Qadir 2:46)
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (commentary) addresses the rule that when making up missed prayers, the condition (travel or residence) at the time of obligation is what matters, not the condition at the time of qada. The author explains: if one missed a short prayer during travel and makes it up when residing, he still prays it short (2 rak'ah). Conversely, if one missed a full prayer during residence and makes it up while traveling, he prays it full (4 rak'ah). The exception is if one intends residence again while traveling—then he shortens the make-up only until that intention. The commentary then quotes Fath al-Qadir to contrast with the case of a sick person: a sick person who cannot stand is permitted to pray sitting, but if he makes up that prayer after recovery, he must stand because the concession was only for the excuse. The traveler's prayer is intrinsically short (2 rak'ah), so the concession is not situational but inherent; hence it remains whether made up in residence or travel.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'ita): A missed prayer that must be made up (qada).
سفر (safar): Travel, legally defined in Hanafi as a journey of at least three days (approx. 78 km) with the intention of travel.
قصر (qasr): Shortening a 4-rak'ah prayer to 2 rak'ah during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 2 rak'ah
B) 4 rak'ah
C) 1 rak'ah
D) It is optional

Key Takeaway
The number of rak'ah in a make-up prayer is fixed to the original obligation; travel or residence at the time of qada does not change it.

Residence in a City: Condition for Obligation of Jumu'ah
Explaining that residence is a condition of obligation, not performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditions of obligation and conditions of performance for Jumu'ah, and apply the Hanafi ruling that residence in a city is a condition of obligation, not validity.

Core Idea
Residence in a city is a condition for Jumu'ah's obligation, not its validity — a traveler who prays it is valid but not obliged.

Friday prayer (Jumu'ah) is a weekly congregation that replaces Zuhr for those who meet specific conditions. This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies a key distinction: some conditions affect whether the prayer is obligatory on a person, while others affect whether the prayer itself is valid. The condition of residing in a city (miṣr) belongs to the first category. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rulings of Jumu'ah to various situations like travel or rural living.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Friday prayer (Jumu'ah). Its ruling is fard 'ayn (individual obligation) for those who meet all conditions of obligation.
The passage uses 'wujub' to mean fard because the evidence is definitive (qat'i).
2
Distinguish conditions of obligation vs. performance
Conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) determine on whom the prayer is obligatory. If missing, one may still pray validly. Conditions of performance (shurut al-ada') determine whether the prayer itself is valid. If missing, the prayer is invalid.
Example: travel is a condition of obligation — a traveler is not obliged but may pray Jumu'ah and it counts.
3
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith: 'Jumu'ah is obligatory except upon a child, a slave, or a traveler' (Bayhaqi). This is the primary proof that residence in a city is a condition.
Consensus of the four imams also supports this.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith exempts the traveler, implying that the non-traveler (resident) is obliged. The traveler is defined as one who is not a resident of the city. Hence, residence is implicit in the obligation.
The word 'wajiba' in the hadith indicates obligation; in Hanafi usul, it can mean fard when supported by definitive evidence.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi detail on the munfasil
Imam Muhammad adds that a person living outside the city (munfasil) who hears the adhan of Jumu'ah becomes obliged. This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
This does not change the condition of residence but extends it by auditory proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The appropriateness of this chapter to what preceded is that Friday prayer resembles the traveler's prayer in number, and since it occurs less frequently, it is appropriate to mention it after it. [2] His saying: 'Condition... etc. Know that Friday prayer requires for its obligation what is required for the obligation of other prayers: Islam, sanity, puberty, purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And additional conditions for its obligation are mentioned by the author here along with some of the general conditions. And other conditions are required for its performance. The difference between these conditions and those is that if all or some of the conditions of obligation are absent, the obligation is not valid, but if one performs it, the performance is valid. However, if the conditions of performance are missing, the performance is absolutely invalid and one must perform Zuhr instead. [3] His saying: 'for its obligation'; what is meant here is fard (compulsory obligation) not the technical wujub, and often wujub is used to mean fard. That is because the obligation of Friday prayer is established by a definitive proof and consensus, not purely by a speculative proof. (4) His saying: 'residence in a city' due to the hadith: 'Jumu'ah is obligatory except upon a child, a slave, or a traveler.' Narrated by Bayhaqi, and upon it is the consensus of the four imams and the majority of scholars, contrary to the Zahiris. So if a traveler performs Friday prayer, it is valid, but it is not obligatory upon him. Thus in al-Ghunya. [0] His saying: 'in a city'; as for one who is separated from it, if he hears the call, Friday prayer is obligatory upon him according to Muhammad, and that is the fatwa. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar from al-Multaqa.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah discusses the conditions of Friday prayer. The author first explains the meaning of the chapter title and its connection to the previous chapter on traveler's prayer (both involve 2 rak'ah). Then he distinguishes between conditions of obligation (shurūṭ al-wujūb) and conditions of performance (shurūṭ al-adā'): if a condition of obligation is absent, the prayer is not obligatory on that person, but if he performs it, it is valid. If a condition of performance is absent, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated as Zuhr. The condition 'residence in a city' is a condition of obligation, not performance. The term 'wujūb' here means fard (compulsory) because the evidence is definitive (qat'i) — the hadith and consensus. The hadith cited (from Bayhaqi) lists exceptions: child, slave, traveler. Thus, Friday is not obligatory on a traveler, but if he prays it, it is valid. The commentary then notes an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Muhammad holds that if a person living outside the city (munfaṣil) hears the adhan, Jumu'ah becomes obligatory on him, and this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
شرط الوجوب (shart al-wujūb): A condition whose absence means the act is not obligatory on the person, but if they perform it, it is valid.
مصر (miṣr): A major city/town that has a ruler, judge, markets, and established congregation for Jumu'ah.
منفصل (munfaṣil): One who lives outside the city but within hearing distance of the adhan of Jumu'ah.
ظاهرية (Zāhiriyya): A school (now extinct) that adheres to the literal meaning of texts and rejects analogy; they differed on the condition of residence for Jumu'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling if a traveler (musafir) performs Friday prayer?
A) It is invalid and must be repeated as Zuhr
B) It is valid but not obligatory upon him
C) It is obligatory and valid
D) It is makruh tahrimi

Key Takeaway
Friday prayer is fard 'ayn on every free, adult, resident male; residence in a city is a condition of obligation, not validity — a traveler who prays it is valid but not obliged.

Salah — Prayer

Conditions for Obligation of Friday Prayer: Distinction and Proofs
List of conditions for obligation with proofs and intra-school differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the obligation of Friday prayer, distinguish them from conditions for performance, and apply the relied-upon Hanafi view on residence.

Core Idea
Friday prayer is fard 'ayn for the resident free adult male; conditions of obligation differ from those of performance.

Friday prayer is a weekly communal obligation with unique conditions. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between what makes it obligatory (shurut al-wujub) and what makes it valid (shurut al-ada). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains these conditions, the proofs behind them, and the intra-school differences on the key condition of residence. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
Identify the act: salat al-jumu'ah (Friday prayer). Determine its hukm level: it is fard 'ayn, not merely wajib, because it is established by decisive proof (dalil qat'i) and consensus, not speculative evidence.
The text explicitly states 'wujub' here means fard.
2
Distinguish conditions of obligation from conditions of performance
The author emphasizes that the conditions listed (residence, health, etc.) are for obligation, not performance. If a condition of obligation is absent (e.g., the person is a traveler), Friday prayer is not obligatory but if performed it is valid. If a condition of performance is absent (e.g., praying behind an imam), the prayer is invalid and must be repeated as Zuhr.
Key Hanafi principle: absence of a shart al-wujub does not invalidate performance; absence of a shart al-ada does.
3
List the specific conditions for obligation
From the text, the conditions include: Islam, sanity, puberty, purity from menstruation/postpartum (for women if obligated), residence in a large town (misr), health, freedom, and masculinity. Each condition is supported by hadith and consensus.
Childhood, insanity, travel, slavery, illness, and being female are exemptions (mawani').
4
Examine the proof for each condition
The primary hadith is: 'Friday is obligatory except for a child, a slave, or a traveler' (al-Bayhaqi) and another: 'Friday is a right obligatory upon every Muslim except four: a male slave, a woman, a child, or a sick person' (Abu Dawud). The exceptions prove that those not excepted are obligated. For health, the exemption includes fears of increased illness; caretakers are also exempt.
The hadiths are ahad (zanni al-thubut) but their indications (dalalah) are strong and reinforced by ijma' of the four schools.
5
Analyse the intra-Hanafi difference on residence
The condition of residence (iqama fi misr) has three views: Muhammad says hearing the call obligates; Abu Yusuf limits obligation to those within the 'boundary of residence' (had al-iqama); the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) from al-Dhakhira and al-Tatarakhaniyya sets the distance at one farsakh. The text adopts Abu Yusuf's view as the most correct.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): a person one farsakh away is obligated; one outside hearing distance and beyond one farsakh is not.

And in al-Dhakhīra and al-Tātārkhāniyya: one between whom and the town there is a distance of one farsakh, Friday prayer is obligatory upon him, and that is the chosen view and upon it is fatwa.
And in Mawāhib al-Raḥmān and its commentary al-Burhān, the view of Abū Yūsuf is corrected: obligation upon one who is within the boundary of residence (ḥadd al-iqāma), meaning that one who leaves it becomes a traveler, and when he reaches it becomes a resident. And it is said in Miʿrāj al-Dirāya that it is the most correct of what has been said on it.
[1] His statement: and health – so it is not obligatory upon a sick person if he is unable to go to the mosque, or is able but fears that his illness will increase or the like, due to the hadith: “Friday is a right obligatory upon every Muslim except four: a male slave, a woman, a child, or a sick person” narrated by Abū Dāwud. Thus in al-Ghunya.
And the caretaker of the sick is included: i.e., one who cares for a sick person if the patient would be lost by his going out, according to the most correct view. Thus in Ḥulbat al-Mujallī.
[2] His statement: and freedom – with a dammah on the ḥāʾ and tashdīd on the rāʾ. So it is not obligatory upon a male slave, whether he is interdicted or permitted to trade. And if his master gives him permission for Friday prayer, it is said that it becomes obligatory upon him; but the more preponderant view is that he still has a choice. And the most correct view is that it is obligatory upon a mukātab and one who is partially freed and forced. Thus in al-Baḥr and al-Sirāj.
[3] His statement: and masculinity – so it is not obligatory upon a woman, and the basis for stipulating freedom is what has passed of the hadith.
[4] His statement: and sanity and puberty – so it is not obligatory upon an insane person or a child, and this is among the general conditions, for nothing is obligatory upon one who is not legally responsible, like a child and an insane person.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conditions for the obligation (wujūb) of Friday prayer, distinguishing them from conditions for its performance (adāʾ). The commentator begins by explaining that the term 'wujūb' here means farḍ (definitive obligation) due to the decisive proof (dalīl qaṭʿī) of Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. The primary condition for obligation is residence in a large town (miṣr), as opposed to a traveler. The hadith cited (from al-Bayhaqi) excludes children, slaves, and travelers. The commentator then clarifies that the 'miṣr' condition is elaborated: according to Muhammad, even one who hears the call from outside the town is obligated. Further views from al-Dhakhīra and al-Tātārkhāniyya set the distance at one farsakh. Abū Yūsuf's view is that obligation applies to those within the 'boundary of residence' (ḥadd al-iqāma), which is considered the most correct. Then the passage lists additional conditions: health (so a sick person is exempt, with inclusion of caretakers), freedom (slaves are exempt, but mukātab and partially freed are obligated), masculinity (women are exempt), sanity, and puberty (general conditions for all obligations). The commentator consistently supports these with hadith and scholarly consensus, noting exceptions and relied-upon positions (muftā bihi) within the Hanafī school.

Key Terms
شرط الوجوب (sharṭ al-wujūb): Condition for the obligation of an act; if absent, the act is not obligatory, but if performed, it is valid.
مصر (miṣr): A large town or city where the Friday prayer is established; typically defined as having a judge (qāḍī) and sufficient population to hold the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Friday prayer is fard 'ayn on every free, healthy, adult, resident male Muslim; the relied-upon Hanafi view for residence is one farsakh from the city mosque.

Conditions for Obligation and Performance of Jumu'ah
Expanding on the conditions for Jumu'ah prayer's obligation and performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions for the obligation of Jumu'ah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the distance threshold, and apply the distinction between conditions for obligation and conditions for performance.

Core Idea
Jumu'ah has distinct conditions for obligation and performance; lacking obligation conditions does not invalidate a prayer you choose to perform.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary on al-Wiqaya details who is required to attend Friday prayer and what makes it valid if they do attend. The author distinguishes between conditions that trigger the duty (wujoob) and conditions that must be met for the prayer itself (ada'). You will also encounter a nuanced debate within the school about how far from the city the obligation extends.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish wujoob from ada'
Identify which conditions the author lists for obligation (health, freedom, masculinity, etc.) and which for performance (being in a misr or its fina). Note that if someone lacking obligation conditions prays, it counts as fard.
The key phrase: 'فتقع فرضاً إن صلاها فاقدها'
2
Examine each obligation condition
Go through the numbered conditions: health (and its exceptions including caregiver), freedom, masculinity, sanity/puberty, soundness of eye, soundness of foot. For each, locate the proof (hadith or Quranic verse).
Pay special attention to the ikhtilaf on the blind person between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the distance dispute
The text cites al-Dhakhira and al-Tatarkhaniyya for one farsakh as the mufta bihi, but then mentions corrections by al-Marghinani and Mi'raj al-Diraya preferring Abu Yusuf's view of hadd al-iqama. Understand what each view means and why the correction exists.
This is an intra-Hanafi tarjih issue – not all later authorities agreed on the fatwa.
4
Understand the performance condition
The sole condition for performance mentioned is that the prayer be held in a misr or its fina'. This is based on ijma' that Jumu'ah cannot be held in deserts or every village.
The fina' (outskirts) is considered part of the city for this purpose.
5
Apply the branching rule
If a person who lacks obligation conditions (e.g., a traveler, slave, sick person) leads the prayer or is present, the Jumu'ah is still valid and counts for them as fard. This follows from the text and is stated in al-Hidaya.
This is a practical consequence of the wujoob/ada' distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة صلاة الجمعة

والصحة، والحرية، والذكورة، والعقل والبلوغ، وسلامة العين، والرجل. فتقع فرضاً إن صلاها فاقدها وإن لم تجب عليه، وشرط لأدائها: المصر، أو فناؤه.

وفي «الذخيرة» و«التاتارخانية»: من بينه وبين المصر فرسخ تجب عليه الجمعة، وهو المختار وعليه الفتوى. وصحح في «مواهب الرحمن» وشرحه «البرهان» قول أبي يوسف بوجوبها على من كان داخل حد الإقامة، أي الذي من فارقه يصير مسافراً، وإذا وصل إليه يصير مقيماً، وقال في «معراج الدراية»: إنه أصح ما قيل فيه.

[1] قوله: والصحة؛ فلا تجب على مريض إذا كان لا يقدر على الذهاب إلى الجامع، أو يقدر إلا أنه يخاف أن يزيد مرضه أو نحو ذلك؛ لحديث: «الجمعة حق واجب على كل مسلم إلا أربعة: عبد مملوك، أو امرأة، أو صبي، أو مريض» أخرجه أبو داود، كذا في «الغنية». ويلحق بالمريض الممرض: أي من يعول المريض إن بقي المريض ضائعاً بخروجه في الأصح، كذا في «حلبة المجلي».

[2] قوله: والحرية؛ بضم الحاء المهملة، وتشديد الراء المهملة، فلا تجب على عبد مملوك سواء كان محجوراً أو مأذوناً له في التجارة، وإن أذن له مولاه لصلاة الجمعة، قيل: تجب عليه، والأرجح أنه مخير عند ذلك أيضاً، والأصح وجوبها على مكاتب ومن أعتق بعضه وأجبر، كذا في «البحر» و«السراج».

[3] قوله: والذكورة؛ فلا تجب على امرأة، والأصل في اشتراطها الحرية ما مر من الحديث.

[4] قوله: والعقل والبلوغ؛ فلا تجب على مجنون وصبي، وهذا من الشرائط العامة، فإنه لا يجب شيء على غير مكلف كالصبي والمجنون.

[5] قوله: وسلامة العين؛ فلا تجب على الأعمى، وإن قدر على قائد متبرع أو بأجرة عنده لعدم اعتبار القدرة بقدرة الغير على أصله، وعندهما إن قدر عليه تجب. وتجب على الأعور وضعيف البصر والظاهر وجوبها على بعض العميان الذي يمشي في الأسواق، ويعرف الطرق بلا قائد من غير كلفة، ويعرف أي مسجد داره بلا سؤال أحد؛ لأنه حينئذ كالمريض القادر على الخروج بنفسه. كذا في «رد المحتار».

[6] قوله: والرجل؛ بكسر الراء، عطف على العين، فلا تجب على المقعد، وإن وجد حاملاً اتفاقاً، كذا في «الخانية»؛ وذلك لأن السعي المأمور به في قوله تعالى: {إذا نودي للصلاة من يوم الجمعة فاسعوا إلى ذكر الله} (الجمعة:9) أي المشي لا يوجد من المقعد. وكذا يتعسر على الأعمى، وقد قال الله: {وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج} (الحج:78).

[7] قوله: فتقع فرضاً؛ يتفرع عليه أنه يجوز إمامة المسافر والعبد والمريض في الجمعة، وتنعقد بحضورهم الجمعة، وإن لم يوجد من سواهم، كما في «الهداية».

[8] قوله: لا لأدائها؛ فإنه لو كانت هذه الشروط شروطاً للأداء لم تصح من فاقدها، كما في الشروط الآتية.

[9] قوله: المصر؛ الأصل فيه أن قوله: {فاسعوا إلى ذكر الله} ليس على إطلاقه إجماعاً بين الأمة، إذ لا تجوز إقامتها في البراري إجماعاً، ولا في كل قرية، فكان خصوص المكان مراد إجماعاً.
Book of Prayer: Friday Prayer. And health, freedom, masculinity, sanity, puberty, soundness of eye, and foot. It occurs as obligatory if one who lacks them prays it even though it is not obligatory upon him, and condition for its performance: the city or its environs. And in al-Dhakhira and al-Tatarkhaniyya: whoever is between him and the city a farsakh, Friday is obligatory upon him, and that is the chosen (position) and upon it is the fatwa. And he corrected in Mawahib al-Rahman and its commentary al-Burhan the statement of Abu Yusuf that it is obligatory upon whoever is within the limit of residence (hadd al-iqama), meaning that whoever leaves it becomes a traveler and when he reaches it becomes a resident. And he said in Mi'raj al-Diraya: it is the most correct of what has been said concerning it. [1] His statement: and health; so it is not obligatory upon a sick person if he cannot go to the mosque, or can but fears that his illness will increase or similar; due to the hadith: 'Friday is a due obligation upon every Muslim except four: an owned slave, a woman, a boy, or a sick person.' Reported by Abu Dawud, as in al-Ghunya. And annexed to the sick is the caregiver: that is, the one who tends to the sick if the sick would be left helpless if he goes out, in the most correct view, as in Halbat al-Mujalli. [2] His statement: and freedom; with damma on the ha and tashdid on the ra, so it is not obligatory upon an owned slave whether restricted or permitted to trade, and if his master permits him for Friday prayer, some say: it becomes obligatory, but the preponderant view is that he has a choice then as well, and the most correct is that it is obligatory upon a mukatab and upon one who has been partially freed and forced, as in al-Bahr and al-Siraj. [3] His statement: and masculinity; so it is not obligatory upon a woman, and the basis for stipulating freedom is what preceded from the hadith. [4] His statement: and sanity and puberty; so it is not obligatory upon an insane person or a boy, and this is from the general conditions, for nothing is obligatory upon one not legally responsible like a boy or an insane person. [5] His statement: and soundness of eye; so it is not obligatory upon a blind person, even if he can find a volunteer guide or for a fee, in his view (Abu Hanifa) because ability through another's ability is not considered in his principle, but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) if he can find one it is obligatory. And it is obligatory upon a one-eyed person and one with weak sight, and apparently it is obligatory upon some blind people who walk in the markets and know the roads without a guide without difficulty, and know which mosque is his without asking anyone; because then he is like a sick person able to go out by himself. As in Radd al-Muhtar. [6] His statement: and foot; with kasra on the ra, conjuncted to eye, so it is not obligatory upon a crippled person, even if he finds a carrier, by agreement, as in al-Khaniyya; because the 'sa'y' commanded in His statement: 'When the call is made for prayer on Friday, then hasten to the remembrance of Allah' (al-Jumu'ah:9) means walking, which is not found from the crippled. And similarly it is difficult for the blind, and Allah has said: 'And He has not placed upon you in religion any difficulty' (al-Hajj:78). [7] His statement: so it occurs as obligatory; it branches from it that it is permissible for a traveler, a slave, and a sick person to lead in Friday prayer, and Friday prayer is valid with their presence even if no one else is present, as in al-Hidaya. [8] His statement: not for its performance; for if these conditions were conditions for performance, it would not be valid from one lacking them, as in the conditions that follow. [9] His statement: the city; the principle for it is that His statement: 'Then hasten to the remembrance of Allah' is not unrestricted by consensus of the ummah, for it is not permissible to hold it in deserts by consensus, nor in every village, so specificity of place is intended by consensus.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the conditions for Friday prayer. The author begins by listing the conditions for obligation: health, freedom, masculinity, sanity, puberty, soundness of eyes, and soundness of feet. Then he mentions that if someone who lacks these conditions prays it, it counts as obligatory (fard) for them, even though it was not obligatory upon them initially. Then he lists the condition for performance: being in a city (misr) or its environs (fina).

There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distance from the city that obligates Friday. Al-Dhakhira and al-Tatarkhaniyya state that whoever is one farsakh (approx. 3-4 miles) from the city must attend, and this is the adopted (mukhtar) position and the fatwa is upon it. However, al-Marghinani in Mawahib al-Rahman and al-Burhan correct the view of Abu Yusuf that obligation applies to those within the 'limit of residence' (hadd al-iqama) — a distance such that leaving it makes one a traveler and arriving makes one a resident. Mi'raj al-Diraya declares this as the most correct.

The commentator then explains each condition:
[1] Health: A sick person who cannot go or fears worsening is exempt, based on the hadith from Abu Dawud. Also exempt is the caregiver of a sick person if the patient would be harmed by his absence.
[2] Freedom: A slave is not obligated, even with permission. A mukatab (slave with contract of manumission) and a partially freed slave are obligated.
[3] Masculinity: Women are exempt, per the hadith.
[4] Sanity and puberty: Minors and the insane are exempt as they are not legally accountable (mukallaf).
[5] Soundness of eye: A blind person is not obligated according to Abu Hanifa, because he cannot be expected to rely on others. But according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), if he can get a guide, it becomes obligatory. A one-eyed person and those with weak vision are obligated. Some blind persons who can navigate independently are also obligated.
[6] Soundness of foot: A crippled person is not obligated, even with a carrier, because the Quranic command 'hasten' (fas'aw) implies walking, which they cannot do. This is based on the verse and the principle of no hardship.
[7] Consequence: If someone who lacks these conditions prays it, it counts as fard. This means they can lead the prayer and their presence makes the congregation valid.
[8] The conditions listed are for obligation (wujoob), not performance (ada'). If they were for performance, someone lacking them could not pray it validly, which is not the case.
[9] The condition of 'misr' (city) is derived from the consensus that Friday cannot be held in deserts or every village. The command in the Quran is not absolute; a specific place is intended.

Key Terms
المصر (al-misr): A city with a governor and judge, where congregational prayers are established. The text states that Friday cannot be held in deserts or every village, so a misr is required.
فناؤه (fina'uhu): The outskirts or environs of the city, extending from its sides.
السعي (as-sa'y): In the Quranic verse, it is interpreted as walking (mashy) to the mosque, not running.
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance approximately 5.5 km (3 miles), used to define the bound for obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, which of the following is a condition for the OBLIGATION (wujoob) of Jumu'ah, but not for its performance (ada')?
A) Being in a city (misr)
B) Soundness of feet (ability to walk)
C) Being a resident (muqim)
D) Hearing the call (adhan)

Key Takeaway
Jumu'ah is obligatory only on free, adult, male, healthy, sane, sighted, able-bodied residents within one farsakh of a city; those lacking may still pray it and it counts as fard.
